People v. Metters


A074986


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


61 Cal. App. 4th 1489; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 294; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 181; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1756; 98 Daily Journal DAR 2445



March 10, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted June 10, 1998 (S069442), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 3667.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Reprinted without change in the January 2001 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Review Denied December 18, 2002, Reported at: 2002 Cal. LEXIS 8624.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County Super. Ct. No. 121038. Joseph W. Jay, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought relief from his conviction in the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) for the robbery of a fast food restaurant Appellant contended at trial that he was forced to commit the crime only because of duress and out of necessity created when drug dealers to whom he owed money threatened to kill him and his family if he did not pay them what was owed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was indicted for robbing a fast food restaurant, and contended he was forced to commit the crime when drug dealers to whom he owed money threatened him and his family. The jury convicted appellant of second degree robbery. As a third-strike offender, appellant was sentenced to 25 years to life imprisonment for the robbery plus 10 years for the prior serious felony enhancements. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence, holding that appellant was not entitled to instructions on the duress defense because he did not commit the crime in response to an immediate threat, nor was he demanded to commit the specific crime charged. Likewise, appellant did not prove the defense of necessity because there were reasonable legal alternatives available to the robbery, and appellant contributed to the circumstances giving rise to the necessity. The court found no error in the use of the standard jury instruction on reasonable doubt, or in the dismissal of a juror who refused to deliberate further, and held that dismissal did not violate appellant's right to a fair and impartial jury. The court held that the sentence was proper under the statutes and was not cruel and unusual.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for second degree robbery, holding that the trial court did not err in refusing to instruct the jury on the defenses of duress and necessity. The court found no error in the jury instruction on reasonable doubt, nor in the dismissal of one juror. The court held that the enhanced sentence was proper and did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.

 

481 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Maribel M. v. Superior Court


No. C028572.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


61 Cal. App. 4th 1469; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 536; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 182; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1780; 98 Daily Journal DAR 2425



March 10, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING: Petition for Extraordinary Writ. Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. JD208313. Alice A. Lytle, Judge. *


* Judge of the Sacramento Municipal Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The petition and request for a stay are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother of minor child filed an extraordinary writ to vacate the order of respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which scheduled a hearing to determine a permanent plan for the minor child under Cal Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26 and terminated reunification services for petitioner, finding that return of the minor to petitioner would create a substantial risk of detriment to the minor.


OVERVIEW: By petition of extraordinary writ under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26, petitioner mother of minor child challenged the decision of the juvenile court, which terminated the reunification services and found that returning the minor to petitioner would create a substantial risk of detriment to the minor, and that the minor likely would be adopted. The court affirmed the decision. The court held that the amendment to Cal. R. Ct. 39.1B was unconstitutional as inconsistent with § 366.26 because the issuance of an order to show cause or an alternative writ created a cause that required a written opinion and was the functional equivalent of an appeal, which was not allowed under § 366.26. The court held that the invalid provisions were severable from rule 39.1B. The court did not deny the petition because it raised issues appropriately disposed of by written opinion. This was not an unusual circumstance in which reunification services should be extended because petitioner had been incarcerated and chose not to avail herself of reunification services, and upon release returned to Mexico. There was no abuse of discretion in finding the minor likely to be adopted.


OUTCOME: The court denied the extraordinary writ of petitioner mother of minor child, holding that the California court rule that allowed appellate courts to decide the petition on the merits by written opinion was unconstitutional as inconsistent with statute. However, the court found that the juvenile court order not to return minor to petitioner was not an abuse of discretion because petitioner chose not to participate in reunification services.

 

482 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Kasler v. Lungren


C017769


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


61 Cal. App. 4th 1237; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 260; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 173; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1581; 98 Daily Journal DAR 2191



March 4, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted May 20, 1998 (S069522), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 3194.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of June 29, 2000, Reported at: 23 Cal. 4th 472.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. CV519977. Ronald B. Robie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff concerned citizens filed an action against defendant attorney general, challenging the validity of the Roberti-Roos Assault Weapons Control Act of 1989, Cal. Penal Code, § 12275 et seq. The Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend, except to one cause of action. Plaintiffs declined to amend and instead sought review of the trial court's decision sustaining the demurrer.


OVERVIEW: The Roberti-Roos Assault Weapons Control Act of 1989 (the Act), Cal. Pen. Code §12275 et seq, consisted of a list by make and model of banned guns. It also included a mechanism by which the judiciary was allowed to add guns to the list. Plaintiff concerned citizens filed an action against defendant attorney general attacking the validity of the Act, especially the add-on provision. A demurrer was filed in plaintiffs' action, and the trial court sustained the demurrer without leave to amend, except as to one cause of action. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the trial court's decision sustaining defendant's demurrer. The court held that the mechanism in the Act that allowed the judiciary to add guns to the list, violated the separation of power doctrine because it required the judiciary to exercise legislative power. The court further found that that Act violated due process, finding that it deprived citizens sufficient notice of the law. Lastly, the court further found that the Act violated the equal protection clause because it did not rationally distinguish between owners of regulated and unregulated guns.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's decision to sustain defendant attorney general's demurrer regarding the Roberti-Roos Assault Weapons Control Act of 1989 (Act). The court held that the Act violated the equal protection clause, because it failed to distinguish between regulated and unregulated guns. It also held that it violated the separation of powers doctrine, by allowing the judiciary to exercise legislative power.

 

483 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Barrett v. Dawson


No. G017070.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


61 Cal. App. 4th 1048; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 899; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 159; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1422; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1943



February 26, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 697494. William F. McDonald, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondents are to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant neighbors sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which declared that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1597.40(c) applied to their action to shut down a home daycare center in their neighborhood pursuant to the subdivision restrictive covenants.


OVERVIEW: Appellee homeowners allowed appellee tenant to operate a daycare center out of their home in a residential subdivision. Appellant neighbors brought an action to have the daycare center shut down because it violated the subdivision's restrictive covenants which had been in effect since the subdivision was developed. The trial court rendered judgment that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1597.40(c), as rewritten in 1983, applied to all restrictive covenants whenever made and mandated that such a covenant could not be enforced against appellees. On appeal, the court affirmed and found that express retroactivity language was not required in the statute where such retroactivity was obviously the intent of the legislature. The court further found that the inability to enforce a covenant was not a taking under the constitution, nor was it violative of the contracts clause because there was a significant and legitimate public purpose for allowing more daycare facilities.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment which declared that a subdivision's restrictive covenants against business activities could not be enforced against home daycare centers under state law.

 

484 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Contra Costa Newspapers v. Superior Court


No. A081220.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


61 Cal. App. 4th 862; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 69; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 142; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1751; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1292; 26 Media L. Rep. 1409



February 20, 1998, Decided  
February 20, 1998


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Super. Ct. No. CR 970199-6. John H. Tiernan, Judge. * 


*  Judge of the Municipal Court for the Colusa Judicial District, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6, of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: We have reached our decision after notice to all parties that we might act by issuing a peremptory writ in the first instance. ( Palma v. U.S. Industrial Fasteners, Inc. (1984) 36 Cal. 3d 171, 177-180 [203 Cal. Rptr. 626, 681 P.2d 893].) The entitlement to relief is obvious and there is also a compelling urgency in light of the forthcoming hearing on real party's new trial motion. (See Alexander v. Superior Court (1993) 5 Cal. 4th 1218 [23 Cal. Rptr. 2d 397, 859 P.2d 96]; Ng v. Superior Court (1992) 4 Cal. 4th 29 [13 Cal. Rptr. 2d 856, 840 P.2d 961].) Accordingly, let a peremptory writ of mandate issue commanding respondeat superior court to vacate its order of June 18, 1997, prohibiting all press contact with the Bishop jurors and to allow the press access to the jurors in the Bishop trial who have been identified in public court records. Nothing in this opinion should be construed as requiring jurors [***2] to speak to the media or anyone else. A juror may speak or remain silent as he or she desires.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner newspaper sought review of an order by respondent Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which prohibited all press contact with former jurors in a criminal trial. Petitioner contended that the order did not satisfy protection for news gathering of U.S. Const. amend. I, and that the order exceeded respondent's authority under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 206.


OVERVIEW: Respondent trial court issued an order prohibiting all press contact with former jurors in a criminal trial. Petitioner newspaper challenged the order, and upon review, the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate commanding respondent court to vacate its order prohibiting all press contact with the jurors and to allow the press access to the jurors who had been identified in public court records. The court reasoned that by its own terms, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 206, granted jurors an absolute right to discuss or not to discuss the deliberation or verdict with anyone. It held that the statute did not purport to allow respondent to prohibit discharged jurors from discussing the case. It also held that respondent could not establish jurisdiction over strangers to the action, and that the issuance of such an order without the further showing of a compelling need impinged on constitutional rights. The court concluded that the order was not directed at anyone in particular, that it was not based on any showing of unreasonable behavior by anyone, and that it was not carefully crafted to restrain conduct while preserving the constitutional rights of those interested in the trial.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate commanding respondent trial court to vacate its order and to allow the press access to the jurors who had been identified in public court records because California law granted jurors an absolute right to discuss deliberations or the verdict with anyone and because the order was unconstitutionally overbroad.

 

485 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Williams


No. H016854.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


61 Cal. App. 4th 649; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 58; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 122; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1150; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1611



February 18, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied May 20, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 3351.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County. Super.Ct.No. SC 940625. John M. Phillips, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County, (California), after a remand for re-sentencing, and claimed that the trial court violated his right to due process by failing to state reasons for adding a year to the sentence previously imposed.


OVERVIEW: After defendant pleaded guilty to residential robbery and receiving stolen property, he was sentenced to 25 years. Under his plea agreement, he could be sentenced to no more that 26 years. Defendant appealed his conviction, and his case was remanded for resentencing, not based upon the merits of his own appeal, but based upon error admitted by appellee state. The trial court resentenced him to 26 years. Defendant filed a second appeal and contended that the trial court had violated his federal constitutional rights by failing to state reasons on the record for imposing the longer sentence, and claimed a presumption of vindictiveness. On appeal, the court affirmed, noting that defendant had failed to object below. The court examined defendant's claims nonetheless and found that the statement of reasons rule was not a constitutional right but a prophylactic rule of presumptive vindictiveness. Because defendant's case had been remanded due to the state's error, and not the trial court's, no presumption of vindictiveness applied. The trial court had, in fact, reduced other portions of defendant's sentence to stay within the sentence limit contained in defendant's plea agreement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the rule requiring a statement of reasons was a prophylactic rule of presumptive vindictiveness which did not apply in defendant's case where the resentencing resulted not from the trial court's error but from appellee state's error. The court noted that the trial court had reduced other portions of defendant's sentence to comply with his plea bargain agreement.

 

486 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Flannery v. California Highway Patrol


No. A076872.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


61 Cal. App. 4th 629; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 632; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 114; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1505; 76 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 212; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1087



February 11, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County. Super. Ct. No. 726290-8. Demetrios P. Agretelis, Trial judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed and the matter is remanded for a reconsideration of the amount of the fee award consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant highway patrol sought the review of the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which entered a jury verdict for and awarded attorney fees based on a multiplier, to plaintiff former officer in plaintiff's suit, which alleged harassment and wrongful termination under the California Fair Housing and Employment Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 12900 et. seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff former officer, after being terminated by defendant highway patrol, filed a suit alleging harassment and wrongful termination under the California Fair Housing and Employment Act (FEHA), Cal. Gov't Code § 12900 et. seq. The lower court entered a general verdict for plaintiff and awarded her expenses and fees, which were based on the multiplication by two of the lodestar amount of reasonable attorney fees pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the award holding that because plaintiff's suit did not benefit a large class of people, as required under § 1021.5, she was not entitled to fees under the statute. The court also held that plaintiff was entitled to fees under FEHA; however, because the lower court had incorrectly based its awarding of the fees on § 1021.5, a new determination of the fees must be made based only on the FEHA.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the order of the lower court, which entered a jury verdict for and awarded attorney fees based on a multiplier, to plaintiff former officer in plaintiff's suit, which alleged harassment and wrongful termination against defendant highway patrol; the lower court applied the incorrect standard in its multiplying of plaintiff's award of attorney fees because the suit did not benefit a large class of people.

 

487 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Californians for Political Reform Found. v. Fair Political Practices Com


No. C026314.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


61 Cal. App. 4th 472; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 606; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 106; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1067; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1441



February 10, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied April 22, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2563.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super.Ct.No. 97CS00170. Cecily Bond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and the postjudgment order awarding plaintiff costs and attorney fees are reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter judgment denying the petition. The Commission shall receive its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Fair Political Practices Commission appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that granted a peremptory writ of mandate in favor of plaintiff political reform foundation in an action that alleged Cal. Code. Regs. tit. 2, § 18215(c)(16) violated Proposition 208.


OVERVIEW: After California adopted Proposition 208, which limited the amount a person could contribute to a political action committee (PAC), defendant Fair Political Practices Commission adopted Cal. Code. Regs. tit. 2, § 18215(c)(16), which restricted the definition of the term "contribution" to exclude payments made for the establishment or administration of a PAC. The trial court found that § 18215(c)(16) conflicted with Proposition 208 and granted plaintiff political reform foundation's petition for a writ of mandate. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's judgment. The court recognized that payments that are not made for political purposes are not contributions, Cal. Gov't Code § 82015. The court held that defendant had the power to adopt and amend regulations to carry out the purpose of the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal Gov't Code § 81000 et seq, and could adopt a regulation that provided that payments made to establish and administer a PAC were not payments for a political purpose. The court held that Proposition 208 did not define the term contribution or limit defendant's power to define the term and found that § 18215(c)(16) was consistent with its purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that granted a peremptory writ of mandate in favor of plaintiff political reform foundation because the regulation adopted by defendant Fair Political Practices Commission did not violate Proposition 208 and was consistent with the purposes of Proposition 208.

 

488 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


STATE v. SUPERIOR COURT OF LOS ANGELES COUNTY


B113661


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


61 Cal. App. 4th 513; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 594; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 108; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1062; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1435



February 10, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Granted March 12, 1998. (See 66 Cal. App. 4th 421)


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in Mandamus. Super. Ct. No. CA000499 c/w Nos. CA001085 & BC165106. Bruce E. Mitchell, Comissioner.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought a writ of mandate directing the Superior Court (California) to vacate its order certifying a class of persons entitled to refund of vehicle license fees and uses taxes on motor vehicles originally sold outside the state which were higher than the fees charged similar vehicles first sold within the state.


OVERVIEW: Respondent resident filed a class action as well as an individual claim against petitioner state, which sought refunds of vehicle license fees and use taxes on motor vehicles originally sold outside of the state, which were higher than the fees charged similar vehicles first sold within the state. The lower court certified and identified two classes of claimants, and the court affirmed. However, the supreme court remanded the action to the court for a decision consistent with the supreme court's holdings. On remand, the court held that the class should have been limited to persons who had filed claims for refunds and that the present claim was only valid to respondent in his individual capacity because there was no statutory provision for class action claims for the refunding of vehicle license fees. The court also held that there was no statutory basis for the refunding of excess use taxes for the portion of the class that remained on that claim. Finally, the court discharged the alternate writ and issued a writ of mandamus ordering the lower court to vacate its previous orders certifying the class and to issue new and different orders consistent with the court's opinion.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternate writ and issued a writ of mandamus ordering the lower court to vacate its previous orders certifying the class and to issue new and different orders consistent with the court's opinion; respondent's class action claim for the refunding of the vehicle license fees was not authorized under state law.

 

489 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Dant v. Superior Court


No. A079050.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


61 Cal. App. 4th 380; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 546; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 99; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 999; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1345



February 6, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of of the City and County of San Francisco County. Super. Ct. No. 837. Lenard D. Louie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As we have previously explained, defendant has been released. Therefore, the alternative writ is discharged, having served its purpose, and the petition is denied as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused sought a ruling to compel respondent, the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), to rule on his motion for release on his own recognizance under Cal. Penal Code § 1270 (a) and Cal. Const. art. I, § 12, following respondent's denial of his petition for writ of mandate, relating to his arraignment on the charge of battery on a cohabitant under Cal. Penal Code § 273.5.


OVERVIEW: The court denied as moot petitioner's request to compel respondent, the trial court, to rule on his motion for release on his own recognizance, after his arrest for battery on a cohabitant under Cal. Penal Code § 273.5, because he had been discharged by the time the court heard his request. The court held that the hearing at which petitioner's motion submitted his motion was required by Cal. Penal Code § 825 and that § 825 required respondent to consider the motion for own recognizance release without unnecessary delay. The court found that respondent erred in delaying its ruling on petitioner's motion, in order to give the state the two day period provided in Cal. Penal Code § 1270.1 to prepare and respond, because respondent was required to consider and rule on the motion once it was presented.


OUTCOME: The court denied as moot petitioner's request to compel respondent, the trial court, to rule on his motion for release on his own recognizance because he was discharged before the court considered the request. Respondent erred in refusing to rule immediately on petitioner's motion because the delay intended to give the state time to prepare a response did not apply in his hearing conducted under Cal. Penal Code § 825.

 

490 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Woods


A076543


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


61 Cal. App. 4th 246; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 511; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 81; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1237; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1303; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 940



February 3, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted May 13, 1998 (S068741), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2961.
Reprinted without change in the Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California Disposition Reported at: 21 Cal. 4th 668.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court, Contra Costa County. Contra Costa County Super. Ct. No. 9614538. Hon. Garrett G. Grant.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged the order of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which suppressed the evidence and dismissed the case against defendants charged with possession of drugs.


OVERVIEW: The lower court granted defendants', charged with drug possession, motion to suppress evidence and the state challenged the order contending that the prohibition against a warrantless residential search pursuant to a probation search condition for the sole purpose of obtaining evidence against a probationer's cotenant was no longer good law. The court affirmed the lower court's order and held that the warrantless residential search pursuant to a probation search for the sole purpose of obtaining evidence against a probationer's cotenant violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court held that the third parties had not waived their right to be free from unreasonable searches and seizures and had a reasonable expectation of privacy in their home. The court held that despite the trend toward ignoring the officer's subjective intent, and asking only whether the facts objectively justified the officer's action, the court would not overrule the prohibition of a warrantless residential search pursuant to a probation search condition for the sole purpose of obtaining evidence against a probationer's cotenant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order that suppressed the evidence and dismissed the case, and held that a warrantless residential search pursuant to a probation search condition for the sole purpose of obtaining evidence against a probationer's cotenant violated the Fourth Amendment.

People v. Hamilton


No. D026836.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


61 Cal. App. 4th 149; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 359; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 73; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 839; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1077



January 29, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing, February 24, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 145. Review Denied April 15, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2379.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. SCD116791. Laura Hammes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted defendant of the lesser-related offense of use of a tear gas weapon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from his conviction of the lesser-related offense of use of a tear gas weapon. Defendant and his companions followed car with defendant's former girlfriend. Defendant allegedly attempted to fire shots at other car, but the gun was a tear gas weapon and inoperable. On appeal, the court affirmed the lower court's judgment holding that it was not an element of Cal. Penal. Code § 12403.7 (a)(8) that the tear gas weapon be loaded or operable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment because weapon possession and use offenses did not require the weapon be loaded or operable.

 

492 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Parker


No. D029756.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


60 Cal. App. 4th 1453; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 167; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 55; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 605; 98 Daily Journal DAR 795



January 22, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied April 29, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2678.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for writ of habeas corpus. Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR134672, Bernard E. Revak and Michael D. Wellington, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause issued in this matter is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus relief is granted to the extent Parker is entitled to a new probable cause hearing which should be conducted consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. The stay order previously entered is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner requested a writ of habeas corpus claiming he was denied a probable cause hearing by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), prior to being held for trial to determine whether he was a sexually violent predator.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of committing a lewd act with a child under that age of 14 and sentenced to prison. Prior to being released on parole, the state filed a petition requesting a determination of whether petitioner was a sexually violent predator as defined in the Sexually Violent Predators Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq., (1996), for the purposes of involuntary commitment. The trial court made a facial review of the pleadings, found probable cause, and set the matter for trial. The petition for writ of habeas corpus was granted to the extent petitioner was entitled to a new probable cause hearing. The court noted that the Act did not establish criminal proceedings but rather civil involuntary confinement proceedings. Because the plain language of the Act referred not only to a facial review of the pleadings but also the right of the petitioner to have an attorney and a "hearing," the court concluded the hearing was to provide petitioner with an opportunity to be fully heard on the issue of probable cause. The court found that petitioner was not afforded due process and was denied a fair probable cause hearing.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition for writ of habeas corpus to the extent that petitioner was entitled to a new probable cause hearing because the Sexually Violent Predators Act required the court hold a probable cause hearing which afforded petitioner with an opportunity to be fully heard on the issue.

 

493 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Prevost


No. B103652.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


60 Cal. App. 4th 1382; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 487; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 48; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 585; 98 Daily Journal DAR 730



January 21, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA086558, George P. Schiavelli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged their conviction in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), of conspiracy to manufacture, distribute, sell and/or advertise cable television signal decoder boxes without the authorization of a franchised or licensed cable television system.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were managers of a business which manufactured and sold equipment used to receive cable television signals. Appellants were not authorized by a franchised or licensed cable television system to manufacture or sell equipment which could descramble cable signals. The court found that Cal. Penal Code § 593d, former subdivision (b) (Statute), was a general intent statute which did not require proof of specific intent to facilitate the theft of cable. The court held that the Statute was constitutional because it was not vague and did not violate the supremacy and commerce clauses of the U.S. Constitution. After reviewing the evidence, the court found it sufficient to support appellants' convictions. Because conspiracy was a separate crime from the underlying offense and had a distinct statute of limitations, the court held that appellants' conspiracy charge was properly governed by the three-year statute of limitations even though the statute of limitations for the underlying offense was one year. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting appellants of conspiracy because the statute was a general intent statute and did not violate the U.S. Constitution, sufficient evidence supported appellants' convictions, and appellants were charged within the appropriate three-year statute of limitations for conspiracy.

 

494 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Pleasant Valley Canal Co. v. Borror


No. F023689.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


61 Cal. App. 4th 742; 72 Cal. Rptr. 2d 1; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 130; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1237; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1716



January 20, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County. Super. Ct. No. 144949. Kenneth E. Conn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Except as to issues not raised on appeal and not discussed in this opinion, the judgment is reversed and is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant ranchers sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which granted declaratory and injunctive relief to plaintiff water company by permanently enjoining defendants from diverting more water than that allocated to them in a previous court decision and from using the water on land other than that described in the prior decision.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff water company sought declaratory and injunctive relief from defendant ranchers' diversion of water from a river via two ditches. The trial court enjoined defendants from diverting more water than had been allocated to them in a previous court decision and from using the diverted water on land other than that described in the previous decision. The court first found that the prior decision was not binding or preclusive as to the parties but that it was persuasive evidence of their historic water use. The court reversed the trial court as to the water diverted from one ditch, because it found that defendants had acquired appropriative rights which were prior and superior to plaintiff's. The court also held that defendants had riparian rights superior to plaintiff's to divert as much water as they were putting to reasonable beneficial uses on land riparian to the river. The court further held that defendants had failed to show that they had acquired any water right with respect to plaintiff by prescription. The court remanded the case to the trial court for a determination of the parties' rights and relative priorities as to water diverted via the second ditch.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court because it found that defendant ranchers had acquired both appropriative rights and riparian rights superior to those of plaintiff water company in the use of water diverted from the river via one ditch. The court remanded the case to the trial court for determination of the nature of the parties' rights and relative priorities as to the water from a second ditch.

 

495 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


In re Tameka C.


A071519


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


60 Cal. App. 4th 1257; 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 471; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 37; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 454; 98 Daily Journal DAR 567



January 15, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 10, 1998. Review Granted April 22, 1998 (S068192), Reported st: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2472.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of January 13, 2000, Reported at: 22 Cal. Cal. 4th 190.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 119674. Robert Dondero, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction and sentence are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that adjudicated her as a juvenile offender guilty of several assaults with a firearm and imposed an enhanced sentence pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5.


OVERVIEW: After shooting a drug dealer who had assaulted her, defendant was ordered by police to drop her gun. Instead, she fired a single shot at the police officers. The bullet discharged by defendant struck a doorway causing a piece of shattered glass to injure the eye of a 6-year old boy sitting nearby. Appellant was adjudicated as a juvenile offender and was found guilty of several charges of assault with a firearm against the police officers and the young boy. The trial court imposed an enhanced sentence for firearm use pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5. Defendant appealed, and the court affirmed. Defendant challenged the sufficiency of the charges related to the young boy because she did not know he was present and did not intend him any harm. The criminal intent required for assault with a weapon was the general intent to willfully commit an act the direct, natural, and probable consequences of which was injury to another. Defendant could not escape the consequences of her actions by claiming she was unaware of the young boy's presence because injury to him was the natural and probable consequence of her assaultive conduct. The sentencing enhancement was properly imposed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's adjudication as a juvenile offender guilty of several charges of assault with a firearm and her enhanced sentence for firearm use were affirmed. It was proper to convict defendant on assault charges for injury caused to the small boy standing nearby even though defendant did not see him because his injuries were a natural and probable consequence of her discharging her gun at police.

Schabarum v. California Legislature


No. C020336.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


60 Cal. App. 4th 1205; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 745; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 29; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 401; 98 Daily Journal DAR 485



January 14, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied April 15, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2338.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV530049, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal. (Cal. Rules of Court, rule 26(a).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) granting a judgment on the pleadings in favor of defendant Legislature and defendant Legislative Counsel on the ground that plaintiff taxpayer's claim based on Cal. Const. art. IV, § 7.5 presented a nonjusticiable political issue. Defendant Legislature filed a protective cross-appeal.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer brought an action alleging that defendant Legislature failed to comply with Cal. Const. art. IV, § 7.5 and the state budget act by excluding defendant Legislative Counsel's budget in defendant Legislature's spending cap. During discovery, defendant Legislature moved for judgment on the pleadings, which the trial court granted on the ground that the case presented a nonjusticiable political issue. Plaintiff taxpayer sought review of the judgment, and defendant Legislature filed a protective cross-appeal. The court affirmed the judgment on the pleadings. Because the action sought an adjudication of the constitutionality of the state budget act, a judicial function, which did not usurp legislative or executive functions, the constitutional question was justiciable. Presuming the constitutionality of defendant Legislature's decision to exclude defendant's budget from its spending limitations unless palpably arbitrary, the court determined that defendant Legislature's provision of a separate budget for defendant Legislative Counsel was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment. The constitutionality of the state budget act was a justiciable question. The political question rule did not compel judgment against appellant taxpayer without consideration of this question. The determination of appellant Legislature to provide a separate budget for defendant Legislative Counsel was not unconstitutional. Judgment on the pleadings was properly granted.

 

497 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hous. Auth. v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. B112610.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


60 Cal. App. 4th 1076; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 738; 63 Cal. Comp. Cases 1; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 28; 98 Daily Journal DAR 504; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 393



January 14, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 25, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review an order of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board. WCAB No. VNO 307018.



DISPOSITION: The Workers' Compensation Appeals Board's order of May 8, 1997, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner housing authority challenged an order of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California) which assumed jurisdiction to determine respondent employee's benefit status and held that petitioner was collaterally estopped to deny respondent's entitlement under Cal. Lab. Code. 4850.


OVERVIEW: The Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (Appeals Board) determined that respondent housing authority police officer was an employee within the meaning of Cal. Lab. Code § 4850, during proceedings on her application for workers' compensation benefits, and determined that petitioner housing authority was collaterally estopped from denying respondent's status. Petitioner sought review, alleging that the Appeals Board had no jurisdiction and had committed error in a prior decision. The court affirmed, finding that the broad duties assigned to the Appeals Board under Cal. Lab. Code § 4851 permitted the inference that it had jurisdiction to decide whether an employee came within § 4850 when it heard the application for benefits. In a prior case, the Appeals Board determined that a housing authority police officer was a police officer entitled to § 4850 benefits. Thus, collateral estoppel barred the court from revisiting that issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the workers' compensation appeals board award of benefits. The appeals board had jurisdiction to determine respondent housing authority police officer's entitlement to disability benefits and collateral estoppel foreclosed reassessing respondent's status as an employee.

 

498 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Landau v. Superior Court


No. A075251, No. A075493.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


81 Cal. App. 4th 191; 97 Cal. Rptr. 2d 657; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 1110



January 12, 1998, Decided * 


* Review granted April 15, 1998. Review was dismissed on June 14, 2000, and the opinion was directed to be partially published.


NOTICE:   [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. ** 


** Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976 (b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III., IV. and VI.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified January 16, 1998. Review Granted April 15, 1998 (S068095), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2217.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Court.
The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Partially Published to Published in Full June 8, 2000.
Review Dismissed and Partial Publication Allowed June 14, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 4856.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 976845. William Cahill, Judge.

Previously Reported at: 60 Cal. App. 4th 940, 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 21, 71 Cal. Rptr. 2d 54 and Withdrawn from the Bound Volumes.



DISPOSITION: The appeal is dismissed. The alternative writ is discharged and the [***2] petition for writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner physician filed a writ of mandate to challenge the judgment of respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which affirmed the decision of real party in interest state medical board to revoke petitioner's license to practice medicine.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest state medical board revoked the license of petitioner physician following administrative proceedings that found gross negligence in her standard of care. Respondent superior court denied petitioner's writ of mandamus, but she filed a writ of mandate pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2327, which provided for appellate review of a superior court decision to revoke, suspend, or otherwise restrict a medical license by extraordinary writ. Petitioner contended that this provision was unconstitutional because it denied her right to a direct appeal. The court reviewed the statute, its legislative history, and its statutory purpose and determined that it was not unconstitutional. The limitation of appellate review of superior court decisions in physician discipline cases to review by extraordinary writ was rationally related to the governmental purposes of reducing delays and expediting the discipline of physicians. Administrative agency decisions accorded for judicial review in a variety of ways. The court also found no abuse of discretion in the license revocation because reasonable minds could differ on the appropriateness of the penalty.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner physician's writ of mandate and affirmed the judgment of respondent superior court, which affirmed the decision of real party in interest state medical board to revoke petitioner's license to practice medicine. Judicial review of an administrative decision to revoke a medical license by way of an extraordinary writ was rationally related to a governmental purpose and not unconstitutional.

 

499 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Lewis v. Superior Court


B115040


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


60 Cal. App. 4th 913; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 598; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 18; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 268; 98 Daily Journal DAR 1195



January 9, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Real Party in Interest's Review Denied April 29, 1998 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2880.


PRIOR HISTORY: ORIGINAL PROCEEDING; petition for writ of mandate. Super. Ct. No. KA023684. Terri Schwartz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Writ granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought a writ of mandate directing respondent, Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), to issue an order directing the clerk of the superior court to accept for filing petitioner's notice of appeal to dismiss petitioner's prior convictions for purposes of the Three Strikes Law.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was sentenced to 25 years to life in prison. The trial court denied the request to dismiss any of petitioner's prior convictions, and petitioner's counsel prepared a notice of appeal. The clerk of the superior court rejected the notice because appeal of the denial of habeas corpus was not appealable at the superior court. The court held that the clerk could not perform any purely judicial function unless specifically authorized by statute. The decision as to whether an arguably appealable order was in fact appealable was a judicial function. Thus, the clerk should not have refused to accept the appeal. Furthermore, the court held that the order denying the dismissal of petitioner's prior convictions was appealable. When the trial court found probable merit in the petition, issued an order to show cause, and conducted a sentencing rehearing, the sentencing decision was considered an order after judgment affecting the substantial rights of the parties, and thus appeal of the decision by respondent was allowed. Therefore, the court issued a writ of mandate directing respondent court to issue an order directing the clerk to accept the notice of appeal for filing.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate directing the respondent court to issue an order directing the clerk of the superior court to accept for filing the notice of appeal because an order to show cause was issued, a rehearing held, and the sentencing decision was considered an order after judgment affecting the substantial rights of the parties and was, therefore, appealable.

Yes on Measure A v. City of Lake Forest


No. G016799.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


60 Cal. App. 4th 620; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 517; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1108; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 38; 97 Daily Journal DAR 21



December 30, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from an order of the Superior Court of Orange County, No. 737126, Ronald L. Bauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Because we hold the city's expenditures incurred in association with the underlying action did not trigger the reporting requirements of the Political Reform Act of 1974, the order granting a preliminary injunction is reversed. The city shall recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed an order from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which issued a preliminary injunction compelling defendant to disclose the expenditures it made in an effort to keep a local economic initiative off the general election ballot.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city filed a pre-election challenge to a keep a local initiate off the general election ballot, alleging that it was legally inadequate and violated various state planning laws. The trial court denied defendant's petition and plaintiffs, local citizen action groups, filed an action seeking a preliminary and permanent injunction compelling defendant to disclose the expenditures it made in its effort to keep the initiative off the ballot. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction, and defendant appealed. The court held that expenditures made by defendant in its pre-election challenge to the legality of the initiative were not reportable within the meaning of the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal Gov't Code § 81000 et seq., because defendant's challenge was not a political act designed to influence voters for or against the measure.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order issuing a preliminary injunction compelling defendant city to disclose expenditures made in an effort to keep a local initiative off the general election ballot was reversed, as the expenditures were not reportable within the meaning of the Political Reform Act of 1974 where defendant's conduct did not constitute political action.

Quackenbush v. Superior Court


No. A078530.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


60 Cal. App. 4th 454; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 271; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1091; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9670; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15463



December 24, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 25, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1963. Certiorari Denied October 5, 1998, 67 U.S.L. Week 3001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 983314, William J. Cahill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue compelling the San Francisco County Superior Court to vacate its injunction and its order overruling Quackenbush's demurrer and to enter a new order sustaining the demurrer without leave to amend. Costs in this proceeding are awarded to Quackenbush.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a state insurance commissioner, state agency director, and state controller, challenged a judgment from respondent Superior Court of San Francisco (California), which entered an injunction when complainants challenged the validity of Proposition 213.


OVERVIEW: Voters approved Proposition 213, a measure that restricted damage recovery in certain automobile injury actions. Several political groups brought an action for an injunction and for declaratory relief, alleging that the measure violated equal protection and due process under the federal and state constitutions and should not have been retroactively applied. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction. Petitioners, a state insurance commissioner, state agency director, and state controller, contended that as economic legislation, the measure was presumed to be constitutional and that its provisions were rationally related to legitimate state interests. On appeal, the court ruled that the measure did not violate due process because applicability did not depend on a factual inquiry requiring a hearing. The court stated that eliminating noneconomic damages for uninsured motorists was rationally related to government interests because it would lead to reduced costs for insurance and to fewer lawsuits. The court also found a lack of standing to challenge the retroactivity of the statute. The court vacated the injunction and ordered the trial court to sustain petitioners' demurrer.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment that entered a preliminary injunction on a challenge to a state initiative restricting automobile accident damages was vacated because the measure did not violate state and federal constitutions and was rationally related to governmental interests.

Trans-Action Commercial Investors v. Firmaterr


No. A075777.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


60 Cal. App. 4th 352; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 449; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1084; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9617; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15525



December 23, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied April 1, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2107.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 748591-8A, John Sutter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The sanctions order is reversed. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Objector attorney challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), granting plaintiffs' motion for mistrial pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 128.5 in a breach of lease action against defendants for objector's repeated and flagrant violations of the in limine orders. Objector was sanctioned under Cal. R. Ct. 227 and was ordered to pay plaintiffs' counsel fees and costs.


OVERVIEW: Objector counsel represented defendant tenants in an unlawful detainer action brought by plaintiff landlords. Defendants operated a historic hotel. Defendants' lease with plaintiffs called for a monthly base rent plus percentages of gross receipts from room rentals, alcoholic beverage sales, and food sales. Plaintiffs argued defendants had violated lease by failing to cause the maximum gross receipts production. Defendants' answer denied any breach of the lease. During objector's opening statement, plaintiffs made numerous objections. Plaintiffs moved for a mistrial, contending objector had engaged in grossly improper argument plainly disregarding the trial court's in limine orders. The jury returned a verdict finding defendants had failed to comply with the lease to operate a restaurant but that the breach was immaterial. Pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 128.5, the trial court granted plaintiffs' motion for mistrial for objector's repeated and flagrant violations of the in limine orders, and objector was sanctioned under Cal. R. Ct. 227. The appellate court reversed the sanctions because Cal. R. Ct. 227 was invalid based on its failure to conform to statutory award conditions.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's sanctions against objector for his repeated and flagrant violations of the in limine orders that caused a mistrial in a breach of lease action between plaintiffs and defendants because the authority of the court's sanction rule to confer a broad sanctioning power on the trial court was inconsistent with the more limited judicial authority to impose sanctions provided by the state legislature.

Hall v. Butte Home Health


No. C023965.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


60 Cal. App. 4th 308; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 246; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1076; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9634; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15354



December 22, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County, No. 118153, William R. Patrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter remanded to the trial court with directions to enter judgment in favor of defendant. Defendant to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant health care facility appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which permanently enjoined its operation of a nonprofit group home for the disabled elderly in a residential neighborhood following an action by plaintiff homeowners.


OVERVIEW: Before defendant began operating a home as a residential care facility, plaintiffs notified defendant that the proposed facility would violate the restrictive covenants in their subdivision. When defendant proceeded with its plans to open the facility, plaintiffs brought an action for injunctive and declaratory relief. The trial court enjoined defendant from operating a residential care facility in the subdivision. On appeal, plaintiffs argued that the trial court's refusal to apply Cal. Gov't Code §§ 12955 and 12955.6 retroactively was compelled by a prior decision, which held that another state law did not invalidate preexisting covenants prohibiting the operation of residential care facilities. The court stated that the disputed statutes forbade discrimination through the enforcement of restrictive covenants prohibiting group homes for the disabled. The court stated that the trial court were aware of the statutes when it rendered the decision. The court held that the statutes did apply retroactively. Additionally, the court held that any impairment on plaintiffs' restrictive covenants was outweighed by the need for adequate housing. The judgment was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment that enjoined defendant's operation of a nonprofit group home for the disabled elderly was reversed and remanded because state law provided that plaintiffs' restrictive covenants could not prohibit the operation of the group home.

 

505 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lopez


H015991


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


60 Cal. App. 4th 275; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 229; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1077; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9641; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15371



December 22, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted April 1, 1998 (S067557), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2094. Review Dismissed September 2, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 5835.


PRIOR HISTORY: Santa Clara County Superior Court. Santa Clara County Super.Ct.No. 187214. Honorable Robert P. Ahern.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California), which convicted him of selling methamphetamine, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379(a). Defendant alleged the admission of unsigned transcript was reversible error, prior convictions were not separate for enhancement purposes, and the sentence was cruel or unusual under Cal. Const. art. I, § 17.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from his conviction for selling methamphetamine, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379(a), enhanced to consecutive 25-year-to-life sentences because of three prior convictions. The court affirmed, holding that his prior convictions qualified as multiple prior felony convictions under Cal. Penal Code §§ 667(b-i), 1170.12, even where they were not brought and tried separately. It was harmless error to admit an unsigned certificated copy of a transcript used to prove the third prior offense because a 50-year-to-life sentence was mandatory on the other two prior convictions. The charged offenses did not arise from the same set of operative facts, because they arose out of entirely separate drug sales. Thus, consecutive terms under Cal. Penal Code §§ 667(c)(6), 1170.12(a)(6) were mandatory. The sentence was not cruel or unusual under Cal. Const. art. I, § 17, because defendant's record showed he would repeatedly reoffend no matter what the sanction, his sentence was proportionate to that which any other recidivist with a like record would have received, and he could have received a life sentence for his offense in several other jurisdictions.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting and sentencing defendant was affirmed because the admission of an unsigned transcript was harmless error, and prior convictions that were not tried separately counted as separate convictions for enhancement purposes. The charged crimes did not arise out of the same set of operative facts, and the sentence was not cruel or unusual under the California constitution.
