NBS Imaging Sys. v. State Bd. of Control


No. C024030, No. C024822.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


60 Cal. App. 4th 328; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 237; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1080; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9638; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15349



December 22, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 18, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1760.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 96SC00148 and 96AS03485, Cecily Bond, Judge, and John R. Lewis, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Sacramento Municipal Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in No. 96CS00148 is reversed and the peremptory writ of mandate is vacated. The superior court is directed to enter a new and different judgment denying the writ petition. The judgment (order) in No. 96AS03485, granting the preliminary injunction, is vacated with directions to enter a new and different order denying NBS's application. Appellants shall recover their costs on appeal in both actions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state agency challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted respondent bidder's petition for writ of mandate and granted a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state agency issued a request for proposals soliciting bids for a turn-key production system to produce drivers' licenses. Respondent bidder lost the contract to another bidder. After the other bidder won the project, respondent tendered a timely protest to the state agency, which forwarded the protest to an administrative board. A hearing officer found that respondent's claims were not supported by the evidence and issued a proposed decision denying the protest because the facts did not support a finding of a conflict of interest. Respondent challenged the decision, and the lower court found that substantial evidence supported the findings of fact. However, the court concluded that the other bidder could not participate in the project. The trial court issued a writ of mandate and rendered the contract invalid. On appeal, the court held that the trial court erred in granting relief to respondent based on a legal theory never presented during the administrative proceedings. The court determined that Cal. Pub. Cont. Code § 10365.5(a) was inapplicable because a consulting component was not included in the bid. The judgment was reversed, and the injunction was vacated.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment that granted respondent bidder's petition for writ of mandate and granted a preliminary injunction was reversed because the trial court erred when it relied on a statute that was inapplicable to the winning bid.

 

507 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Guevara v. Superior Court


H016326


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


60 Cal. App. 4th 261; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 220; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1068; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9597; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15506



December 19, 1997, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Granted January 16, 1998.
Rehearing Opinion of March 27, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 264. (See 62 Cal. App. 4th 864).


PRIOR HISTORY: Santa Cruz County Superior Court. Santa Cruz County Superior Court. No. S6-09574. Honorable Robert Yonts. Honorable William Kelsay.



DISPOSITION: Petitioner's request for relief from the Superior Court's denial of his demurrer denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought to vacate an order of the Santa Cruz County Superior Court (California). The trial court denied motions by petitioner, who was charged with unlawful sexual intercourse, failing to register as a sex offender, and aggravated assault, to dismiss the assault counts and to dismiss allegations under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.85, a sentence enhancement statute for HIV-positive perpetrators.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner had unprotected consensual sexual intercourse with a minor without disclosing that he was HIV-positive. Petitioner was charged with unlawful sexual intercourse, failing to register as a sex offender, and aggravated assault. Petitioner moved to dismiss the assault counts and to dismiss allegations under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.85, a sentence enhancement statute. The trial court denied both motions. The court directed the trial court to dismiss the assault counts. There was no evidence a single incident of unprotected sex between an HIV-positive male and uninfected female was likely to result in the transmission of HIV antibodies. The court upheld the denial of petitioner's demurrer to section 12022.85. Section 12022.85 did not violate equal protection even though it enhanced the punishment for sex crimes where the perpetrator knew he was HIV-positive; no other communicable diseases posed the same deadly threat to sex crime victims. That section 12022.85 only applied where the victim was female also did not violate equal protection; an HIV-positive male who had sexual intercourse with a minor female risked infecting not only the female victim but also any of her offspring.


OUTCOME: The court directed the trial court to dismiss the assault counts against petitioner, who had unprotected sexual intercourse with a minor, because there was no evidence that one incident of unprotected sex would transmit HIV antibodies. The court upheld the constitutionality of a statute that enhanced the punishment for sex crimes where the perpetrator knew that he was HIV-positive; the statutory distinctions did not violate equal protection.

 

508 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Lechuza Villas West v. California Coastal Com


No. B105571, No. B110008.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


60 Cal. App. 4th 218; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 399; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1074; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9571; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15277



December 19, 1997, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts C.4., C.5., C.6., and C.7


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Petition for Rehearing January 14, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 44. Review Denied April 1, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2126.Certiorari Denied October 5, 1998, 67 U.S.L. Week 3234.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment and order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC076855, Ernest M. Hiroshige and Robert H. O'Brien, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The stay order heretofore issued shall be lifted upon issuance of the remittitur by this court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant coastal commission sought review from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found error in the denial of certain land permits to respondent landowner and found liability regarding respondent's takings claims. Appellant asserted, among several other issues, that the permits were properly denied and the trial court judge made an erroneous determination that appellant engaged in a taking.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landowner purchased certain real property with the intention to develop it. Appellant coastal commission ultimately denied certain permits necessary for the development of the land due to the unresolved status of boundary issues. Respondent brought an action regarding the denial of the permits and asserted that appellant was engaged in a taking by the actions. Appellant sought review after the trial judge ruled in favor of respondent. Appellant asserted that the permits were properly denied and the trial court judge made an erroneous determination that appellant engaged in a taking. On review, the court found that, because respondent had not established the location of a boundary line and the location of an express easement before applying to appellant for permits, appellant's denial of the permit applications was appropriate. The court therefore reversed the decision in favor of respondent and the court ordered the trial judge to vacate his order granting summary adjudication in favor of respondent on the issue of takings liability.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision in favor of respondent landowner and ordered the trial judge to vacate his order granting summary adjudication in favor of respondent on the issue of takings liability. The court found that appellant coastal commission's denial of the permit applications was appropriate because respondent did not establish the location of a boundary line and the location of an express easement before applying for the permits.

 

509 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court


No. B109959.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


60 Cal. App. 4th 202; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 388; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1073; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9582; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15492



December 19, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied April 1, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 2137 


PRIOR HISTORY: PETITION for writ of Mandate/Prohibition. Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A795977 and ZM002140, Harold E. Shabo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ having served its purpose is discharged. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondeat superior court to vacate its pretrial order declaring Blakely's diagnosis as a matter of law does not qualify as a mental disease, defect or disorder under section 1026.5, subdivision (b)(1), and to proceed to trial on the People's petition for extended commitment, guided by the principles set forth herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought a writ of mandate directing respondent judge of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), to vacate its order declaring that real party in interest criminal's diagnosis of Axis II antisocial personality disorder did not qualify as a mental disease, defect, or disorder within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 1026.5(b)(1), and to proceed to trial on its petition for extended commitment of real party in interest.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest criminal pled guilty to charges of inflicting corporal injury upon his spouse and assaulting his mother-in-law. Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1926, respondent judge of the trial court ordered real party in interest confined by the department of mental health. Three months prior to the conclusion of the sentence, the state filed a petition for extension of the commitment by reason of a mental disease, defect, or disorder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1026.5(b)(1). Respondent granted real party in interest's pretrial motion seeking a determination of whether his diagnosis of antisocial personality disorder constituted a mental disease, defect, or disorder within the meaning of § 1026.5, and respondent held that, as a matter of law, it did not. The court granted the state's request for a writ of mandate directing respondent to vacate the pretrial order and to proceed to trial on the state's petition for extended commitment. The court held that the determination was a question of fact to be resolved with the assistance of expert testimony. Further, respondent's ruling that the lack of effective treatment precluded any extended commitment was also contrary to law.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state's petition for writ of mandate directing respondent judge to vacate its order declaring that real party in interest criminal's diagnosis of antisocial personality disorder did not, as a matter of law, qualify as a mental disease, defect, or disorder for purposes of extended commitment. The court directed respondent to proceed to trial, where the determination was a question of fact.

 

510 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Four Point Entertainment, Inc. v. New World Entertainment, Ltd.


No. B106261.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


60 Cal. App. 4th 79; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 82; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1055; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9490; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15231



December 18, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC128731, Richard P. Kalustian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal is dismissed, and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to vacate the judgment and the stipulation on which it is based. The parties are to pay their own costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant producer sought review of a summary adjudication from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), granted to respondent entertainment company on appellant's tort claims. Appellant had also sued for breach of contract, and the parties stipulated to a dismissal of all remaining claims. They stated an intention to appeal and stated that an appeal did not prejudice any right to prosecute claims voluntarily dismissed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant producer and respondent entertainment company had a contract. Appellant sued respondent on a tort theory and a breach of contract theory. Respondent moved for summary judgment, and the trial court denied it but entered summary adjudication on the tort points in favor of respondent. The parties then entered into a stipulation, which voluntarily dismissed all the claims not adjudicated by the trial court and stated an intention to appeal. The stipulation also said that any appeal did not prejudice either parties' right to prosecute voluntarily dismissed claims. Appellant sought review. The court dismissed the appeal and remanded the case to the trial court to vacate the stipulation. The court held that the parties could not create appellate jurisdiction by using a stipulation. To allow such an appeal would have violated the final judgment rule, which did not allow an appeal to be taken from a judgment that failed to complete the disposition of all causes of action between the parties. The court held further that a writ of mandate was the appropriate vehicle for interlocutory review and that it was not appropriate in this case.


OUTCOME: Ruling in favor of respondent entertainment company and against appellant producer, the court dismissed the appeal and remanded the case to the trial court with directions to vacate the judgment and the stipulation on which it was based. The court held that the final judgment rule did not allow parties to create appellate jurisdiction by stipulation, where none existed.

 

511 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Trinkle v. Stroh


No. C024840.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


60 Cal. App. 4th 771; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 661; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1125; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 225; 98 Daily Journal DAR 233



December 18, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published January 7, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from an order of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 274098, William J. Murray, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. ABC shall recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, vending machine owners, sought review from the order from the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which denied their action, under Cal. Penal Code § 335a, to recover jukeboxes and pinball machines seized by respondent alcohol beverage control agency as illegal slot machines and lotteries under Cal. Penal Code § 330. Appellants contended the law was vague and the machines were exempt.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, vending machine owners, brought an action under Cal. Penal Code § 335a to recover jukeboxes and pinball machines seized by respondent alcohol beverage control agency as illegal gaming devices. The trial court entered judgment in favor of respondent and appellants sought review, contending that the statute was vague, that the machines were exempt, and the pinball machines were antiques and not subject to the statute. On review, the court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent. Vagueness was not an issue because the jukeboxes had already been adapted to award free songs and cash, which was an illegal use by appellants. The machines were not exempt due to the element of chance used in winning free songs and cash, and appellants had not sustained their burden of proof under the statute that demonstrated the pinball machines were antiques and not subject to forfeiture.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed denial of appellant vending machine owners' action seeking to recover jukeboxes and pinball machines. The alleged vagueness of the statutory language was irrelevant to the appeal because the jukeboxes were already adapted to a prohibited use, and flipper-less pinball machines were not exempt due to the element of chance used with a cash reward.

 

512 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Sdc/Pullman Partners v. Tolo


No. G016212.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


60 Cal. App. 4th 37; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 62; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1062; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9512; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15239



December 18, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County, No. 684137, Richard O. Frazee, Sr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lessor sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which directed a verdict in favor of respondent former lessee in appellant's action to recover the cleanup costs for toxic and hazardous substances. Appellant contended that it was respondent's duty to clean up for the substances which he placed on the premises.


OVERVIEW: Appellant lessor brought an action against respondent former lessee, arguing that a toxic substance clause in the lease obligated respondent to clean up all toxic and hazardous substances at the property, regardless of the fact that the amount of the wastes did not warrant governmental legal action. The trial court directed a verdict for respondent. On appeal, appellant asserted that the presence of detectable amounts of various chemicals precluded a directed verdict. The court disagreed and affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding that the trial court's reading of the toxic substance clause was correct. The court determined that the terms and circumstances of the toxic substance clause pointed to the conclusion that the toxic substance clause was intended to protect appellant against actual liability, or at least the realistic threat of actual liability, from respondent's noncompliance with environmental law. The court held that the clause did not require respondent to spend potentially enormous sums to extract trace and de minimis amounts of certain molecules to avoid purely speculative environmental liability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a directed verdict in favor of respondent former lessee in appellant lessor's action to recover the cleanup costs for toxic and hazardous substances where the trial court properly interpreted the toxic substance clause in the lease. The court held that the clause was intended to protect appellant from actual liability from respondent's noncompliance with environmental law and not from purely speculative liability.

People v. Superior Court (Pipkin)


No. B091707, No. B092243.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


59 Cal. App. 4th 1470; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 180; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1041; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9402; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15097



December 15, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING. Petitions for Writ of Mandate. Super. Ct. Nos. VA022226 and GA020808. Robert W. Armstrong and Terry Lee Smerling, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let peremptory writs of mandate issue setting aside the probationary orders in these cases. The respondent courts are to set forth their reasons for striking the prior serious felony findings in writing pursuant to Penal Code section 1385, subdivision (a).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought writs of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to set aside its order that struck respondent convicts' prior felony convictions, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 667(c) - (i) and 1170.12. The state supreme court had remanded with instructions that respondent court vacate its prior decision that set aside respondent convicts' prior convictions and reconsider in light of new case law.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state filed for two writs of mandate and moved to set aside two orders of respondent superior court that set aside serious felony convictions of respondent convicts, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667(c)-(i) and Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12, after the state supreme court remanded the case back to the court with instructions that the court vacate its prior order that directed respondent to set aside respondent convicts' felony convictions and reconsider the case. The court issued the writs of mandate and set aside its prior order and held that respondent court did not set in writing its reasons for setting aside respondent convicts' serious felony convictions, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385(a). The court held that respondent court's order to strike respondent convicts' prior convictions had to proceed in strict compliance with Cal. Penal Code § 1385(a) and that respondent's order was subject to review for abuse. The court found that respondent court's written statement of its reasons for striking respondent convicts' prior serious felonies was subject to appellate review.


OUTCOME: Writs of mandate were issued and an order was issued to set aside prior orders. Respondent court was instructed to set in writing its reasons for setting aside respondent convicts' prior serious felony convictions because respondent court was required to do so by statute, because respondent court's actions was subject to review for abuse, and because respondent court's power to strike prior convictions was limited.

McBrearty v. City of Brawley


No. D027877.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


59 Cal. App. 4th 1441; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 862; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1037; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9408; 97 Daily Journal DAR 15104



December 15, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Imperial County. Super. Ct. No. 90380. Matias R. Contreras, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the superior court issuing the writ of mandamus is affirmed as modified. The matter is remanded to the superior court for the purpose of ordering the City either to hold an election on the tax within 90 days of this decision becoming final or to cease collecting the tax upon the expiration of that 90-day period.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review an order of the Superior Court of Imperial County (California), which was in favor of respondent citizen, that issued a writ of mandamus to compel appellant to submit a proposed utility tax to a vote, pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 53723, within four months.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city challenged the trial court's order that issued a writ of mandamus that required appellant to submit a new utility tax to a vote pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 53723, within four months. Appellant asserted that the statute of limitations had run on respondent citizen's petition, that the statute was unconstitutional, and if constitutional, it should not be applied retroactively. The court modified, affirmed, and permitted appellant to either hold an election or cease collecting the tax, because the statute of limitations had begun when the state supreme court established a duty for appellant to conduct an election on the new tax. The court noted that prior to the decision, respondent had no chance to compel adherence to Proposition 62. The court held that the statute was constitutional under Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 24 because voter approval was a permissible condition placed on the exercise of local taxing authority. The court found that state supreme court's decision was retroactive and because appellant failed to demonstrate that retroactive application would have imposed a financial hardship, retroactive application of the statute was permitted.


OUTCOME: An order of the trial court was modified and affirmed because the statute of limitations did not begin to run until a state supreme court decision established a duty. Because appellant city failed to show financial hardship and a vote to approve a new tax was a permissible condition placed on the local taxing authority, retroactive application was permitted.

Lake Cadena Invs. v. City of Colton


E017900


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


60 Cal. App. 4th 1046; 70 Cal. Rptr. 2d 654; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1131; 98 Cal. Daily Op. Service 367; 98 Daily Journal DAR 472



December 15, 1997, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published January 13, 1998.
Review Denied March 11, 1998 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1603.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County. Super. Ct. No. SCV273124. Raul S. Rosado, Judge. *


* Retired Judge of the Superior Court, sitting under assignment by the Chief Justice pursuant to art. VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed from a judgment entered in the Superior Court, San Bernardino (California), in favor of plaintiff mobile home park owner. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in ruling that a 1992 amendment to defendant's rent stabilization ordinance, Colton Mun. Code § 15.48.040, unconstitutionally impaired existing contractual rights and was preempted by Cal. Civ. Code § 798.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city appealed a judgment in favor of plaintiff mobile home park owner, asserting that the trial court erred in ruling that 1992 amendment to defendant's rent stabilization ordinance unconstitutionally impaired existing contractual rights and was preempted by Cal. Civ. Code § 798. The amendment, Colton Mun. Code § 15.48.040, prohibited mobile home parks from requiring prospective tenants to assume existing long term leases. The ordinance also provided that prospective tenants could voluntarily assume leases, Colton Mun. Code § 15.48.040(D), that all rights of Cal. Civ. Code § 798.17 would apply, and that no tenant would be required to accept a long term lease as a condition of tenancy, Colton Mun. Code § 15.48.680(A). On appeal, in de novo review, the court held that the ordinance was preempted where a conflict existed with general laws, because 1992 amendment made a portion of a remaining long term lease subject to local rent control when the lessee assigned the lease to another party. The new tenant could accept the terms or enter an entirely new lease. The court held that this was in conflict with Cal. Civ. Code § 798.17(a), that provided exemptions from rent control.


OUTCOME: The court held that the 1992 amendment to defendant city's rent stabilization ordinance was preempted by state law. The amendment made the remaining portion of a long-term lease subject to local rent control when an original lessee assigned the lease to another party in conflict with the state civil code. The court affirmed the judgment for plaintiff mobile home park owner.

 

516 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Arron C.


No. A076469.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


59 Cal. App. 4th 1365; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 852; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1033; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9316; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14977



December 11, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied January 2, 1998. Review Denied March 25, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1956.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Contra Costa Superior Court No. 9502647. Lawrence Katz, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The disposition is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of a disposition of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which denied appellant's motion to suppress and placed him on probation for six months pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 for the offense of receipt of stolen property in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 496(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile challenged the decision of trial court that denied his motion to suppress and placed him on probation for six months after his premises were searched by a probation officer who mistakenly believed that appellant was still on probation pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 for a prior offense and found stolen items in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 496(a). The court affirmed and found that evidence that was erroneously seized by a probation officer was not subject to the exclusionary rule. The court held that the probation officer was a part of the juvenile court and noted a line of cases that held that the exclusionary rule did not apply to evidence seized as a result of actions by court officials. The court found that the exclusionary rule was designed to curb police misconduct and noted that there was no evidence that court officials were inclined to ignore or subvert the U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court did not find any basis for the assertion that the application of the exclusionary rule would have made court officials more responsible. The court noted that court officials had no stake in the outcome of criminal prosecutions.


OUTCOME: A disposition that placed appellant juvenile on six months probation was affirmed because evidence of stolen items obtained as a result of an erroneous search of appellant's premises by a probation officer was not subject to the exclusionary rule, because probation officers were an arm of the court and not the police, and because there was no evidence that the exclusionary rule would deter unlawful behavior by the probation officer.

 

517 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



First Presbyterian Church v. City of Berkeley


No. A078481.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


59 Cal. App. 4th 1241; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 710; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1023; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9243; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14925



December 10, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing January 7, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 12.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County. Alameda County Super. Ct. No. 769451-0. Mark L. Eaton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with respect to the 2d, 10th, 12th and 13th causes of action insofar as it holds the City is barred by the Ellis Act from exercising discretionary review of the Church's application for a demolition permit under the NPO, LPO and CEQA with respect to the historic or aesthetic value of the subject Building and Property, without any regard to considerations as to future construction of residential units on the Property, the habitability of the Building sought to be demolished, or the provision and maintenance of rental housing in the City. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and city council, sought review of a judgment entered in the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted declaratory and injunctive relief and issued a writ of mandate in favor of respondent church, in respondent's action for a demolition permit under appellant's neighborhood landmarks preservation ordinances.


OVERVIEW: Respondent church applied for a demolition permit for a residential building under appellants', city and city council, neighborhood preservation ordinance (NPO). The Landmark Commission declared the building a "landmark" under the landmark preservation ordinance (LPO) requiring an environmental impact report (EIR). Respondent gave notice to its tenants, under Cal. Gov't. Code § 7060 et seq. (the Ellis Act), that it was removing the building from the residential market. Appellant city council affirmed the landmark designation and denied the demolition permit. Respondent petitioned for a writ of mandate. The trial court ruled that the Ellis Act preempted appellant city's permit procedures under the NPO, the LPO, negated the EIR requirement, and guaranteed the grant of a demolition permit. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed in part and held that the Ellis Act preempted the LPO and the NPO where they interfered with the ability of a landlord to remove units from the rental market. The court also reversed in part and held that the Ellis Act did not preempt the ability of appellant city to deny a demolition permit under its police powers with respect to land use.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and held that the Ellis Act preempted appellants', city and city council, permit procedures when they interfered with respondent church's ability to take residential property off the market. The court reversed in part and held that the Ellis Act did no interfere with appellants' ability to regulate demolition permits under its police powers.

 

518 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Goodwin


No. B110092.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


59 Cal. App. 4th 1084; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 576; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1008; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9204; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14807



December 8, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied January 5, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 39. Review Denied March 18, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1736.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. LA024881. Darlene E. Schempp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, and the trial court is directed to amend the abstract of judgment to reflect the imposition of the $ 2,000 restitution fine.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of commercial burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459 and petty theft with a prior in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 666. The lower court found that defendant had suffered two prior convictions for serious or violent felonies within the meaning of the Three Strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his convictions for commercial burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459, and petty theft with a prior, Cal. Penal Code § 666. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions. The court held that the trial court's practice of referring to jurors by their juror identification numbers, rather than by name, did not violate Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 237 because the trial court and counsel were not barred from access to juror identifying information. The practice of using juror identification numbers at trial did not deny defendant his right to a public trial under U.S. Const. amend. VI & XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 15 because there was no constitutional requirement that jurors' names must have been spoken in the courtroom and defendant's trial was open to the general public at all times. The court held that defendant's sentence of 25 years to life, which was imposed under the Three Strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667(b)-(i), was not cruel or unusual punishment under U.S. Const. amend. VIII because defendant's record revealed an unrelenting pattern of criminal conduct and the sentence was not disproportionate to sentences imposed under recidivist statutes in other jurisdictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions, holding that the jurors were not anonymous because their names were known to the trial court and counsel, that defendant was not denied his constitutional right to a public trial because the trial was open to the general public at all times, and that defendant's sentence of 25 years to life under the Three Strikes law was not cruel or unusual.

 

519 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Kucera v. Lizza


No. A075859.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


59 Cal. App. 4th 1141; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 582; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1009; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9190; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14787



December 8, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied January 7, 1998. Review Denied February 25, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1315.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the of Marin County. Super. Ct. No. 162731. Gary W. Thomas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff building owners appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which rendered judgment in favor of defendant building owner on the action plaintiffs brought under Tiburon, Cal., Code ch. 15, to compel defendant to restore plaintiffs' views, which had become obstructed by the growth of defendant's trees. The trial court held that the view obstruction ordinance was unconstitutional and void.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff building owners brought an action under a local ordinance, Tiburon, Cal. Code Ch. 15, to compel defendant building owner to remove trees that were obstructing plaintiffs' views and sunlight. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's judgment that was rendered in favor of defendant. The court held that the view and sunlight obstruction ordinance was constitutional because regulating tree growth was a reasonable means of achieving the valid police power goals of preserving views and sunlight. The court did not address the issue of whether the city could declare the unreasonable obstruction of views and sunlight by trees a public nuisance because the court already held that the ordinance was valid under the city's general police power. The court held that the ordinance was not preempted by either the general law on easements or the general law on solar easements because the ordinance did not contradict the general laws nor did it enter an area fully occupied by general law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment entered against plaintiff building owners and remanded the case for further proceedings because the trial court erred in finding that the ordinance was void. The court held that the regulation of tree growth was a valid exercise of the city's police power to preserve views and sunlight and that the ordinance was not preempted by state law governing easements.

People v. Tran


H016119


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


59 Cal. App. 4th 1125; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 535; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 998; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9116; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14701



December 4, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 30, 1997. Review Granted March 11, 1998 (S067199), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1461.
Reprinted without change in the Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Review Dismissed November 10, 1999 and cause remanded to Ct. App., 6st Dist. (rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct., Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7813.


PRIOR HISTORY: Santa Clara County Superior Court. Santa Clara County Super.Ct.No. 188651. Honorable Paul Teilh.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a conviction by the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California), of attempted murder with premeditation, two counts of assault with a firearm, and using a handgun in the crimes. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in giving an improper jury instruction, and in doubling his sentence, which imposed two consecutive life sentences, under Cal. Penal Code § 667.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of attempted murder with premeditation, two counts of assault with a firearm, and using a handgun in the commission of the crimes. Defendant had a prior conviction within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 667 and Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12, and was sentenced to two consecutive indeterminate life terms and a consecutive nine-year determinate term. The court found that the trial court did not err in failing to give Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 8.72 sua sponte because the instruction given, Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 17.10 was sufficient, and that Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 2.90 was not constitutionally deficient. The court found the trial court did err in imposing two consecutive life terms under § 667. In the court's view, the trial court was authorized by § 667 to set the minimum parole ineligibility period and to double that, not to double the sentence which might result in less time served, contrary to the intent of the statute. The court affirmed the conviction and modified the sentence to a nine-year determinate term and a consecutive indeterminate life term, with no eligibility for parole for 14 years.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed as modified to change the two life terms to an indeterminate life term, with no parole eligibility for 14 years, for attempted murder with premeditation, assault with a firearm, and using a handgun in the crimes. The court held that the jury instructions given were sufficient, and that the enhancement statute authorized the doubling of the minimum parole ineligibility period, rather than doubling the sentence.

 

521 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Owens


No. B109675.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


59 Cal. App. 4th 798; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 428; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 973; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8965; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14423



November 26, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied December 23, 1997. Review Denied February 25, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1358.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County. Super. Ct. No. 38800. Steven Z. Perren, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), that convicted him of a felony charge of operating an endless chain scheme, under Cal. Penal Code § 327. The trial court declared the conviction a misdemeanor and determined that it was permissible for the prosecutor to distinguish between defendant, as a police officer, and civilians in charging decisions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a felony charge of operating an endless chain scheme, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 327. The trial court declared the conviction to be a misdemeanor pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 17(b). Defendant was a police officer and the trial court found that defendant's status motivated his prosecution and the felony charge. Defendant appealed and argued that it was unconstitutional discrimination to selectively prosecute him over civilians. The court affirmed the judgment and held that police officers were not a suspect class for equal protection purposes because, inter alia, there was no constitutional right or fundamental interest in continued public employment. Moreover, there was no fundamental right to pursue one's chosen profession or to personal liberty. Consequently, a rational basis test applied. Because participation in the crime interfered with the discharge of defendant's obligations as a police officer, there was a rational basis for selective prosecution. Deterrence provided an additional rational basis. The court found nothing invidious about the prosecution in this case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction for operating an endless chain scheme. The court held that defendant's status as a police officer did not trigger a strict scrutiny review, but only a rational basis review. The court found that there was a rational basis for the selective prosecution of police officers for the crime, as it interfered with the discharge of their duties.

 

522 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Crystal R. v. Superior Court


No. H016859.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


59 Cal. App. 4th 703; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 414; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 974; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8977; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14439



November 26, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified, December 16, 1997. Review Denied February 18, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1124.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County. Super. Ct. No. J015341. Kathleen K. Akao, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner de facto parents of the real party in interest child sought a writ of mandate from an order of respondent judge of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), to allow their family ties to become permanent and legal.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest child was born to an Indian father and a non-Indian mother who were unable to provide for the real party in interest, who was cared for by relatives who were non-Indian. In this writ, petitioner de facto parents sought an order from respondent judge to allow their family ties to become permanent and legal. In opposition to the petition, the biological parents contended that respondent had to apply the requirements of the Indian Child Welfare Act (ICWA), 25 U.S.C.S. § 1902, before terminating parental rights. Congress declared that the policy behind the ICWA was to protect the best interests of Indian children and to promote the stability and security of Indian tribes and families. This was to be accomplished in part by the establishment of minimum federal standards governing the removal of Indian children from their families. The court granted the writ petition and return the matter to respondent so that a hearing could be held to determine whether there was factual support to establish that the real party in interest was part of an existing Indian family, so as to justify the application of the ICWA.


OUTCOME: The writ of petitioner de facto parents was granted and respondent judge was directed to conduct a hearing in order to determine whether the father, who was Indian, maintained significant ties with the tribe in order to apply the Indian statute at issue to the custody proceedings of real party in interest child.

 

523 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Caro v. Smith


No. G016245, No. G016461.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


59 Cal. App. 4th 725; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 306; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 975; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8972; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14433



November 26, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 24, 1997. Review Denied March 11, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1614.


PRIOR HISTORY: .Appeal from a judgment and post-judgment order of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 652170. Nancy Wieben Stock, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in No. G016245 is affirmed. The judgment in No. G016461 is affirmed as modified to award Caro the additional amount of $ 2,234.20 for statutory costs. In No. G016245 Caro is awarded sanctions against Attorneys Jeffrey H. Baraban and James S. Link jointly in the amount of $ 12,250, and they and the clerk of this court are each ordered to forward a copy of this opinion to the State Bar upon return of the remittitur. ( Bus. & Prof. Code, § 6086.7, subd. (c) & 6068, subd. (o)(2).) Caro shall have costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant employer appealed an order from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), where the arbitrator's award was confirmed and plaintiff employee was awarded judgment interest.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee brought suit against defendant employer for injuries she sustained while on the job. Plaintiff sued in tort because defendant lacked workers' compensation coverage. Plaintiff subsequently dismissed her action after the parties entered into an arbitration agreement. The arbitrator entered judgment in plaintiff's favor and awarded her attorney fees. The arbitration order was confirmed by the trial court and the trial court awarded plaintiff judgment interest. Defendant appealed asserting that it did not consent to arbitration. The court affirmed the judgment. According to the court, defendant was deemed to have consented to the arbitration because he appeared and participated without objecting. The court held that defendant could not object at this point in time to avoid the unfavorable award of attorney fees. The court viewed defendant's counsel's attempt to void the arbitration judgment as deplorable because defendant was not deceived about the arbitration. Thus, the court awarded attorney fees as a sanction for the frivolous appeal. Next, the court held that the award of judgment interest was proper because the judgment was confirmed by the trial court.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed the judgment confirming the arbitration award because defendant employer was not deceived into participating in the arbitration. The court held that although defendant did not sign the arbitration agreement, he ratified the agreement by appearing and participating.

California Teachers Assn. v. State of California


C019678


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


59 Cal. App. 4th 516; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 261; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 960; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14333; 13 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 882; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8871



November 24, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted February 18, 1998 (S067030), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 918.
Reprinted without change in the Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California disposition reported at: 20 Cal. 4th 327.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super.Ct.No. CV376695. James T. Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state controller challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which found unconstitutional, as a violation of respondent teacher's U.S. Const. amend. XIV due process rights, a portion of Cal. Educ. Code § 44944(e) that required respondent teacher to pay costs of a hearing on professional competence.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state controller had notified respondent teacher that he was responsible under Cal. Educ. Code § 44944(e) for half of the costs of his hearing to determine professional competence, and that under Cal. Gov't Code § 12419.5, respondent's tax refunds would be withheld. Respondent filed a petition for a writ of mandate and a complaint for declaratory relief, arguing that the statute placed an undue burden on his due process right in seeking an initial opportunity to be heard. The trial court found the statute unconstitutional. On appeal, the court examined the state interest as compared with respondent's property interest, and found that respondent had a protected interest in his tenured position. Further, the court held that because the competence hearing was respondent's initial opportunity to be heard, the statute, which imposed an open-ended burden on respondent if he didn't prevail, did not comply with respondent's U.S. Const. amend. XIV due process rights. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's ruling that the challenged statute was unconstitutional, holding that because respondent teacher had a property interest in his tenured position and the hearing on professional competence was his only recourse upon termination action, it was the government's duty to provide an opportunity to be heard. The threat of financial burdens failed to comply with respondent's Fourteenth Amendment due process rights.

 

525 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Connell v. Superior Court


No. C024295.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


59 Cal. App. 4th 382; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 231; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 948; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8821; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14255



November 20, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied February 25, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. Nos. CV347181, CV357155, CV357156, CV357950. James T. Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the trial court to vacate its judgment and enter a new judgment denying the Districts' petitions for writ of mandate. Appellants shall recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted petitions for writ of mandate brought by real parties in interest, water districts. The dispute was whether a statewide regulatory amendment, increasing the level of purity of reclaimed wastewater, constituted a state-mandated program for which real parties in interest were entitled to reimbursement from appellant.


OVERVIEW: The trial court determined that real parties in interest, water districts, were entitled to reimbursement from appellant state for a state-mandated program increasing the purity of reclaimed wastewater. The court concluded the judgment was interlocutory but treated it as a writ petition. The court found that the issues presented were not limited to the validity of any finally adjudicated individual claim, but encompassed the question of subvention obligations in general under the regulatory amendment of wastewater purification standards. The court found that taxpayers statewide would suffer unjustly the consequences of a continuing obligation to fund the costs of local water districts if the decision was incorrect. Therefore, the court concluded that even assuming collateral estoppel was present that it should be disregarded pursuant to the public interest exception, insofar as appellant's contentions presented questions of law. The court concluded that the real parties in interest were not entitled to reimbursement of state-mandated costs, because they had authority to levy fees sufficient to pay for the level of service mandated by the regulatory amendment.


OUTCOME: The court directed the trial court to vacate its judgment and enter a new judgment denying real parties in interest, water districts' petitions. The court concluded the real parties in interest's authority to levy fees defeated their claim of a reimbursable mandate, and appellant state was not collaterally estopped from raising the matter.

Cel-Tech Communications, Inc. v. Los Angeles Cellular Telephone Co.


B094578


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


59 Cal. App. 4th 436; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 207; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 950; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8807; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14235; 1997-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P71,985



November 20, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted February 3, 1998 (S066735), Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 487.
Reprinted without change in the Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Suprem Court of California disposition reported at: 20 Cal. 4th 163.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. VC015535. C. Robert Simpson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part and remanded, and affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff cellular telephone companies challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which held that defendant cellular telephone company did not violate the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 16720, commit unfair business practices under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17200, § 17043 and § 17044, or intentionally interfere with prospective economic advantages by selling below cost cellular phones to the public.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sold cellular phones to the general public below costs, in order to gain a share in the cellular phone market. Plaintiffs, cellular phone businesses, in direct competition with defendant filed suit, arguing that defendant violated antitrust laws under the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 16720, committed unfair business practices under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17200, § 17043 and § 17044, and intentionally interfered with prospective economic advantages. The trial court found in favor of defendant, and plaintiffs challenged the ruling. The court held that defendant did not violate antitrust laws or intentionally interfere with prospective economic advantages, because antitrust laws specifically exempted defendant's actions, when it involved cellular phones. The court also determined that defendant did not commit unfair business practices under § 17043 or § 17044 because there was no intent that defendant intended to hurt competition. However, the court determined that defendant's actions may have violated § 17200, even though its actions were legal. The court reversed in part, and remanded for further determination of whether defendant's actions violated § 17200.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgment of the lower court, holding that defendant did not violate antitrust laws or intentionally interfere with prospective economic advantages by selling below cost cellular phones to the public. However, the court determined that defendant may have committed an unfair business practice by selling the phones, and remanded for further proceedings.

Runnion v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. A077286.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


59 Cal. App. 4th 277; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 105; 62 Cal. Comp. Cases 1511; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 934; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14109; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8732



November 18, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] WCAB Case No. WCK 022473.



DISPOSITION: The Board's contempt award is annulled. The Board's sanctions are sustained. Each party is to bear his/its own costs in this proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought review of the contempt order and sanctions issued against him by respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California) after he failed to appear at a hearing on a discovery dispute in a case he was handling against respondent employer. Petitioner claimed he was not guilty of contempt and argued lack of authority to issue the contempt order, and objected to the sanctions.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney received a notice of a hearing concerning a discovery dispute in a case before respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (Board), but he did not appear and he gave no notice to the workers' compensation judge (WCJ). After a hearing, the WCJ found he had no valid excuse, fined him $ 250 for contempt and added sanctions, including respondent employer's costs for the hearing on discovery. Cal. Lab. Code § 5309 provided that a WCJ could issue all necessary process in proceedings for direct and hybrid contempt in a like manner and to the same extent as courts of record, which were governed by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209. However, the court found that respondent Board's regulations would have caused any reasonable attorney confusion about the obligations to appear for conferences and hearings and the consequences of failure to appear. Although the WCJ lacked the authority to hold petitioner in contempt when it was not made clear his presence was mandatory, she could properly sanction him for bad faith actions and for trifling with the proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court annulled respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board's contempt award against petitioner attorney and sustained its sanctions for bad faith in failing to respond to notice of a hearing on respondent employer's discovery request. The court explained that the record did not show that petitioner knew or recklessly avoided learning that his appearance was compulsory and absence would have been contempt.

 

528 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



City of San Bernardino Hotel/Motel Ass'n v. City of San Bernardino


No. E017944.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


59 Cal. App. 4th 237; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 97; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 936; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8735; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14117



November 18, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied February 3, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 583.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County. Super.Ct.No. 264661. John P. Wade, Judge. **


** Judge of the Municipal Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to art. VI, § 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The Association is to recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hotel/motel association appealed the decision of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) that defendant city's ordinance imposing a transient occupancy tax was constitutional in a suit it brought challenging it. Plaintiff argued that the ordinance was preempted by Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 7280 and that it was void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff hotel/motel association filed a suit to have defendant city's ordinance imposing a transient occupancy tax declared unconstitutional, but the trial court upheld the ordinance. The court rejected plaintiff's argument that the ordinance was preempted by Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 7280 which gave cities the power to levy occupancy taxes on rooms occupied for less than 30 days. The complained of ordinance taxed transient use for less than 90 days. Noting that defendant was a charter city, the court found the ordinance fell within the city's powers. However, the court reversed the decision because it found the ordinance unconstitutionally vague, explaining that the definitions of hotel and transient were circular. The court also held the definitions did not adequately distinguish between a person living from day-to-day or week-to-week in a motel and an apartment dweller on month-to-month tenancy. Applying the test that an ordinance which required the doing of an act in terms so vague that men of common intelligence must necessarily guess at its meaning and differ as to its application, the court found the ordinance violated due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision in the suit brought by plaintiff hotel/motel association that defendant city's transient occupancy was constitutional, explaining that the terms used were so vague that it violated due process of law. However, the court ruled that the city had the authority to levy that type of tax and noted that defendant was free to try again, with appropriate modifications.

People v. Moore


No. F025271.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


59 Cal. App. 4th 168; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 56; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 927; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8669; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14039



November 14, 1997, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified December 18, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County. Super. Ct. No. 63382. Roger D. Randall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which convicted him of conspiracy to misappropriate public funds, conspiracy to obtain money by false pretenses, and grand theft by false pretenses. Appellant was sentenced to a total unstayed term of five years in prison and ordered to pay restitution.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of crimes, where he and a superintendent of a water district billed the district for materials that were never delivered. The court found that the district was a water district formed pursuant to the California Water District Act, which conclusively established the district as a district of the State of California. Therefore, the court concluded that the trial court was entitled to take judicial notice that the district was a "district" for purposes of misappropriating public funds. The court found that for appellant to be guilty of conspiracy to misappropriate public funds, the superintendent had to be an officer of a district of the state. The court found that did not mean that the trial court removed an element of the offense from the jury's consideration by instructing the jury on the matter of which it took judicial notice. The court affirmed and concluded that the trial court did not instruct that the superintendent was an officer of a district of the state but merely instructed that the district was a "district" of the state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted appellant of conspiracy to misappropriate public funds, conspiracy to obtain money by false pretenses, and grand theft by false pretenses. The court found that the trial court appropriately took judicial notice of a fact and did not remove an element of an offense from the jury's consideration.

 

530 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Cod Gas & Oil Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. C024659.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


59 Cal. App. 4th 756; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 366; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 979; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9022; 97 Daily Journal DAR 14531



November 12, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published December 1, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super.Ct.No. 95AS06154. Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff retailers challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) sustaining defendant state board's demurrer without leave to amend for plaintiffs' failure to state a cause of action in plaintiffs' tax refund case.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff retailers collected use taxes from motor fuel purchasers, including supplemental county taxes later declared unconstitutional. Through Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 7275, et seq., the legislature authorized refunds of the unconstitutional tax to purchasers, excluding retailers. Plaintiffs sought refunds and were denied. Plaintiffs then filed a court action seeking the refunds and a declaration that § 7275 was unconstitutional. The trial court sustained defendant state board's demurrer, without leave to amend, for plaintiffs' failure to state a cause of action. Plaintiffs appealed. The court affirmed, holding that it was a requirement per Cal. Const. art. XIII § 32 that actions for refunds of illegal taxes be brought only in the manner prescribed by the legislature, and § 7275 was clear in its exclusion of retailers from the process. The court also rejected plaintiffs' argument that § 7275 operated ex post facto to deprive them of a vested right, and the distinction favoring purchasers over retailers for refunds was justified based on a legitimate state interest in directly refunding invalid taxes to purchasers as the persons who bore the economic burden of the tax.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the statute was clear in its exclusion of the retailers from the illegal tax refund process and did not operate ex post facto to deprive plaintiff retailers of a vested right. The favoring of purchasers over retailers for refunds of the illegal tax was justified based on a legitimate state interest.

Mission Housing Development Co. v. City & County of San Francisco


No. A075003.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


59 Cal. App. 4th 55; 69 Cal. Rptr. 2d 185; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 917; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8631; 97 Daily Journal DAR 13981



November 12, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing December 12, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 1053. Review Denied March 11, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Cal. LEXIS 1482.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Superior Ct. No. 920940. William Cahill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in part (with respect to tax years 1982-1983 through 1984-1985 and 1987-1988), reversed in part (with respect to tax years 1985-1986 and 1986-1987), and the matter remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. Costs to appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) in upholding the San Francisco Assessment Appeals Board decision denying a number of applications for reduction in tax assessment filed by appellants.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers sought review of an order upholding the San Francisco Assessment Appeals Board (AAB) decision denying appellants' applications for reduction in tax assessment. The court affirmed in part the trial court. The court found that the AAB failed to hear and make a final determination on their applications within two years after they were filed, as required by Cal. Revenue and Taxation Code §1604(c) and, therefore, appellants were entitled to have their opinions of value, as stated in their applications for reduction in assessment, inserted on the assessment roll for the tax years during which they awaited a hearing but only for certain years. The court also concluded that based on the language of Cal. Revenue and Taxation Code § 5097, appellant's refund claims were untimely for the certain years and affirmed the trial court's judgment with respect to those tax years. The court also found that appellants properly exhausted their administrative remedies, that the assessor's method of assessment was not arbitrary and that, while appellants were not entitled to attorney's fees under § 5152, they may be entitled to such fees under Cal. Gov. Code § 800.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the trial court's order and remanded the case for further proceedings with respect to the issue of attorneys fees.
