In re Marriage of Rebecca & David R.


E013860


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


54 Cal. App. 4th 471; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 730; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 309; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2919; 97 Daily Journal DAR 5105



April 21, 1997, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: July 30, 1997 the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4949.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Superior Court of Riverside County. Super.Ct.No. D-121563. Richard G. Van Frank, Judge.

Original Opinion of June 14, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Cal. App. LEXIS 552.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of an order entered by the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) in connection with proceedings to dissolve his marriage to respondent wife. The trial judge ordered the husband to pay child support for two minor children pursuant to Cal. Fam. Code § 7540, even though blood tests established that he was not the biological father of the children.


OVERVIEW: During dissolution of marriage proceedings, appellant husband was ordered to pay child support for two children born during his marriage to respondent wife. Respondent later informed appellant that he was not the biological father of the children. The lower court ordered a blood test that confirmed that another man was the biological father. When appellant sought to avoid paying child support, respondent argued that Cal. Fam. Code § 7540 established a conclusive presumption that the child of a wife cohabiting with her husband who was not impotent or sterile was a child of the marriage. In reversing the trial court's order, the court ruled that the blood tests rebutted the presumption that appellant was the father. In addition, Cal. Fam. Code § 7541, which required a father to request blood tests within two years of a child's birth, was not relevant, because the tests were not requested by appellant but had been ordered by the trial court. Although the trial judge had the discretion to decline to order blood tests, once he had done so he had no discretion to ignore the results.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court requiring appellant husband to pay child support for the two children. The court held that the two-year limitation on filing motions for blood tests was not applicable to tests ordered by the court, and that based on the results of the blood tests obtained the trial court erred in ordering appellant, who was not the biological father, to pay child support.

 

590 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (Mudge)


No. B107385.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


54 Cal. App. 4th 407; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 721; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 302; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2890; 97 Daily Journal DAR 5055



April 18, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 9, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. F242899, Michael L. Duffy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. The request for a peremptory writ is denied. The stay order is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prosecution sought a peremptory writ in response to an order entered by respondent, the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California). Petitioner had stipulated with the real party in interest defendant pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.65(a) that the retired judge assigned to their case was not qualified. The presiding judge did not allow the challenge and declared that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner prosecution and real party in interest defendant stipulated that the retired judge assigned to hear and try defendant's case was not capable or qualified. The presiding judge disallowed the challenge and declared that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.65(a) was unconstitutional. The statute provided that a retired judge should not hear and try any criminal cause when the prosecution and defense jointly stipulated that the retired judge was not capable or qualified. In affirming the trial judge, the court ruled that by allowing the parties to a criminal action to veto the chief justice's assignment, the legislature had substantially impaired the justice's constitutional power in violation of Cal. Const. art. III § 3.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's request for a peremptory writ and affirmed the order of the trial court. The court agreed that the civil procedure code substantially impaired the chief justice's constitutional power to appoint retired judges whom he determined were "capable and qualified." The statute was therefore unconstitutional.

 

591 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Bernardi v. City Council


B098962


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


54 Cal. App. 4th 426; 63 Cal. Rptr. 2d 347; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 307



April 18, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Review Denied June 11, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Super. Ct. No. C136398 & C133468.

Original Opinion of March 19, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 197.
Bernardi v. City Council, 53 Cal. App. 4th 718, 61 Cal. Rptr. 2d 895, 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 197 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., 1997)



DISPOSITION: Petition for rehearing denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city council petitioned for rehearing of an order of a superior court (California), which denied appellant's request for modification of a final judgment that imposed restrictions on appellant's redevelopment plan, claiming that modification was authorized based on changed circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Respondent individual brought an action in rem to invalidate a redevelopment plan by appellant city council. The trial court entered a final judgment based on a consent decree that, by stipulation, imposed certain fiscal and time parameters on the plan. Appellant sought to modify the judgment and petitioned for rehearing of the order denying the modification. The court denied the petition, holding that contrary to appellant's contention, the proceeding brought by respondent was simply an action in rem, which was not transformed into an in personam proceeding by the final judgment. Further, the fiscal cap and time limitations were adjudicated by the stipulated judgment, and therefore the restrictions on the plan were binding and conclusive under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. §§ 870 & 33503. Finally, the modifications that appellant sought were based upon a change in factual circumstances, contrary to the authority cited by appellants that allowed modification of a stipulated judgment based on a change in the law.


OUTCOME: The court denied appellant city council's petition for rehearing. The judgment entered by the trial court imposing restrictions on appellant's redevelopment plan was not subject to modification where the restrictions were binding and conclusive, and appellant provided no authority for modification based on changed factual circumstances.

Ojavan Investors v. Cal. Coastal Com


No. B089152.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


54 Cal. App. 4th 373; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 803; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 313; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2862; 97 Daily Journal DAR 4997



April 17, 1997, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II, III and IV of Discussion.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied July 9, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4244. Certiorari Denied December 8, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 7364, 66 U.S.L. Week 3398.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from orders and a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC159661. Ruth Essegian, Judge. *


* Judge of the Minicipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Appellants to pay respondents' costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant investors challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which imposed civil fines and issued a permanent injunction against appellants for their violation of deed restrictions which were entered into by prior landowners as a condition of respondent California Coastal Commission's grant of coastal development permits.


OVERVIEW: Appellant investor purchased 54 lots, which had deed restrictions that stipulated the land should be recombined into two larger parcels and not subdivided. After appellant sold 19 of the lots, respondent California Coastal Commission brought an action to undo the sales and return the property back to its original two tracts as required by the deed restrictions. The trial court granted respondent's request for recission of the sales, enjoined any further partition, and fined appellant a total of almost $ 9.5 million. Appellant challenged the judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed and found that Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 30803, 30820, 30821, provided relief to restrain any violation of the Coastal Act or an order inconsistent with a previously issued permit, and provided for civil fines for violations. The court found that appellant was bound by the covenants in the deeds and that he knowingly violated the Coastal Act 73 times and refused to remedy the violations. The court concluded that because of the egregiousness of appellant's actions, the fines imposed were proportional to the number of violations and appellant's flagrant disregard of the commission's land development scheme.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment against appellant investors because appellants knowingly subdivided the land in violation of the California Coastal Act's goal of limiting development and in violation of the restrictive covenants in the deeds of the property, and after being notified of the violations, refused to remedy them.

 

593 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gallegos


No. F025107.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


54 Cal. App. 4th 252; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 666; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 291; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2802; 97 Daily Journal DAR 4903



April 15, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied July 9, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4389.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County, Super. Ct. No. 63928. Richard J. Oberholzer and Sidney P. Chapin, Judges. *


* Judge Oberholzer was the sentencing judge; Judge Chapin ruled on the suppression motion.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which granted a motion to suppress, permitted the prosecution to dismiss the case, refile the charges and oppose a subsequent motion to suppress without a binding effect of the first ruling under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, which allowed the state, following the grant of a motion to suppress, to dismiss the case, refile it, and oppose a subsequent motion to suppress, without binding effect of the first ruling. The second motion to suppress was denied. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court and found that § 1538.5 was constitutional. The court held that no new evidence presented, the second motion was submitted on transcript from the first hearing, it was heard by the same judge and therefore the state could not have been forum shopping. The court found that the discrepancies between the suppression rulings appeared to have stemmed from a more complete review of legal precedent the second time, which the state would have been entitled to had they appealed the initial suppression ruling under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(o). The court further found that the statute was not unconstitutional on its face and found that the clear and unambiguous language of the statute provided the state with one free dismissal in the context of a motion to suppress which did not violate any of defendant's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court because it was constitutional for the state to get one free dismissal in the context of a motion to suppress, and because defendant was not restricted in the second matter as to what witnesses could be called, what evidence could be presented, and he was free to rely on or supplement his previous arguments.

 

594 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Adames


No. B101822.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


54 Cal. App. 4th 198; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 631; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 286; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2753; 97 Daily Journal DAR 4854



April 14, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied April 29, 1997. Review Denied June 25, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 3866.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment and an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. MA008966. Ronald S. Coen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and order are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment and an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (Cal.), which convicted him of continuous sexual abuse of a child, Cal. Penal Code § 288.5, and required him to submit to testing for AIDS pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1202.1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of continuous sexual abuse of a child, Cal. Penal Code § 288.5, and was required to submit to AIDS testing. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed the conviction and held that the absence of a requirement that the jury unanimously agree on the particular incidents of abuse underlying a violation of § 288.5 did not violate appellant's U.S. Const. amend. VI right to a jury trial or his U.S. Const. amend. XIV guarantee of due process. The court also held that the transmission of genital herpes was a proper aggravating factor justifying the imposition of an upper term of imprisonment. The court further held that the AIDS testing mandate of Cal. Penal Code § 1202.1 applied to the offense for which appellant was convicted. The continuous-course-of-conduct crime did not require jury unanimity on a specific act, because it was not the specific act that was criminalized. Transmission of genital herpes involved great bodily harm. The sexual offense listed § 1202.1(e)(6) as lewd or lascivious acts with a child in violation of § 288 encompassed a violation of § 288.5 which was based on three or more acts of lewd or lascivious conduct under § 288.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of continuous sexual abuse of a child because a jury did not need to unanimously agree on the particular incidents underlying appellant's crime, the transmission of genital herpes was a proper aggravating factor justifying the imposition of an upper term of imprisonment, and an AIDS testing mandate applied to the offense for which appellant was convicted.

 

595 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



L.A. Lincoln Place Investors v. City of L.A.


No. B101751.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


54 Cal. App. 4th 53; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 600; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 271; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2603; 97 Daily Journal DAR 4547



April 7, 1997, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III(C) and (D).


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied July 9, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4278.
Review denied by Los Angeles Lincoln Place Investors v. City of Los Angeles, 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4278 (Cal., July 9, 1997)
Related proceeding at Lincoln v. City of Los Angeles, 2005 Cal. App. LEXIS 1092 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., July 13, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. SC038778. Alan B. Haber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Plaintiffs, Los Angeles Lincoln Place Investors, Ltd., and Elkgrove Investors, Ltd., shall recover their costs incurred on appeal from defendant, City of Los Angeles.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant municipality challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared defendant's ordinance regarding demolition permits unconstitutional as it applied to plaintiff landowners. Plaintiffs cross-appealed for a finding that the ordinance was unconstitutional on its face rather than only as to them.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landowners applied for a demolition permit for a portion of their property which contained rental units. Defendant city refused to grant a permit unless plaintiffs agreed to limit the use of the property to rental units for the next ten years, pursuant to Los Angeles Municipal Code § 91.0303(a)(5). Plaintiffs filed suit, claiming that the ordinance was unconstitutional and in violation of the Ellis Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 7060, et seq. The trial court found the ordinance unconstitutional as applied to plaintiffs, and defendant appealed. Plaintiffs cross-appealed, arguing that the ordinance was unconstitutional on its face. The court affirmed the trial court. The court determined that the Ellis Act prohibited a municipality from formulating any rule, procedure, or ordinance which had the effect of compelling the owner of real property to offer that property to rent or lease. The court held that the effect of the ordinance in plaintiffs' case was to compel them to continue to use their property for rental purposes. The court also held that plaintiffs were not required to demolish all of their contiguous rental units for the demolition to be subject to the Ellis Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court declaring the ordinance unconstitutional as it applied to plaintiff landowners, and held that the Ellis Act prohibited ordinances which worked to compel a property owner to continue to offer his property for rent or lease.

 

596 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Pham v. Nguyen


G019481


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


54 Cal. App. 4th 11; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 422; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 267; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2565; 97 Daily Journal DAR 4493



April 4, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 733856. Super. Ct. No. 733857. Thomas N. Thrasher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant dentist appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied defendant's individual request for a continuance based on the unavailability of an expert witness under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 595.2 and denied defendant and plaintiff patient's stipulated request for a continuance based on a need to depose witnesses, in an dental malpractice action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patient filed a dental malpractice suit against defendant dentist. Four days before trial, the parties filed, and the trial court denied, a stipulated request for a continuance based upon the need to depose expert witnesses and defendant's individual request for a continuance under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 595.2 based upon the unavailability of an expert witness. Defendant contended that the trial court abused its discretion by denying the continuance requests. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment and held § 595.2 was directory not mandatory and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying the stipulated and individual request for a continuance. The court found the trial court was not required under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 2024(f) to grant a continuance pursuant to an agreement to extend time for completion of discovery and the stipulation failed to invoke § 595.2; and while the unavailability of a witness was considered good cause, the facts were insufficient to explain the witness' unavailability, failing to show whether the witness was subpoenaed and whether the witness' absence was an unavoidable emergency.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment which denied the parties', plaintiff patient and defendant dentist, stipulated request and defendant's individual request for a continuance and held the trial court did not abuse its discretion because the parties were not entitled to a continuance to complete discovery; and defendant failed to show the absent witness was subpoenaed or his unavailability was due to an unavoidable emergency.

 

597 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Nowlin v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. B100375.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


53 Cal. App. 4th 1529; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 409; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 262; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2518; 97 Daily Journal DAR 4421



April 2, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Modified April 30, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Super. Ct. No. BS035547. Robert O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The matter is reversed. Each party to bear its own costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner California Department of Motor Vehicles challenged a judgment of a Superior Court (California), which granted respondent drivers' petition for writ of mandamus prohibiting petitioner from requiring social security numbers for drivers' license applications under Cal. Veh. Code § 12800, and from disclosing social security numbers to other state agencies under Cal. Veh. Code § 1653.5(f)(1), (2), and (3).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner California Department of Motor Vehicles required applicants for drivers' licenses to obtain and disclose their social security numbers under Cal Veh. Code § 12800, and petitioner shared that information with other state agencies under circumstances set forth in Cal. Veh. Code § 1653.5(f)(1), (2), and (3). Respondent drivers sought a writ of mandamus to prohibit this activity, which the trial court granted. On review, the court held that 42 U.S.C.S. 405(c)(2)(C)(i), was an exception to the Privacy Act of 1974, 42 U.S.C.S. 552a, because it permitted states to require persons to provide social security numbers for certain uses, including drivers' licenses and motor vehicle registration. Thus, petitioner could require applicants for new and renewal drivers' licenses to obtain and provide social security numbers, and it could share the information with other agencies as statutorily required. The court held that respondents lacked standing to bring a petition for writ of mandamus under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1086, because they were not beneficially interested parties as the provisions requiring disclosure of the social security numbers did not apply to them.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which granted a writ of mandamus to respondent drivers in their action to prohibit petitioner California Department of Motor Vehicles from requiring applicants for drivers' licenses to disclose their social security numbers and to share such information with other agencies. Petitioner was required by state law to get such information, and the requirement was not subject to the Privacy Act of 1974.

 

598 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Speegle


No. C021838.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


53 Cal. App. 4th 1405; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 384; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 258; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2468; 97 Daily Journal DAR 4286



March 31, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing was Denied April 24, 1997. Review Denied July 9, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 4331.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the judgment of the Superior Court of the County of Butte. Super.Ct.No. CM003371. William R. Patrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The conviction for animal neglect (§ 597f) is reversed. The judgment is otherwise affirmed in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Superior Court of the County of Butte (California), which convicted her of eight counts of felony animal cruelty in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 597b, and one count of misdemeanor animal neglect in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 597f, and ordered her to pay for the costs of impounding the animals. Defendant asserted that Cal. Penal Code § 597, was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of eight counts of felony animal cruelty, Cal. Penal Code § 597b, and one count of misdemeanor animal neglect, Cal. Penal Code, § 597b. On appeal, the court held that Cal. Penal Code § 597, was not unconstitutionally vague because it gave fair notice of an objective standard of reasonableness in the provision of sustenance, drink, and shelter, and in the avoidance of infliction of suffering. The court held that the fact that defendant had to assess the point at her conduct became criminally negligent did not violate due process. The court held that even if the confiscation of the animals was only a forfeiture, civil forfeitures were not punishment for purposes of double jeopardy. The court held that § 597f, required proof of criminal negligence, and the court's failure to so instruct the jury required reversal of the misdemeanor conviction. The court held that the trial court did not err in failing to give a lesser included offense instruction because § 597f, was only lesser in penalty. The court held that § 597f, allowed impoundment officers to recover costs for animals other than the direct victims and for costs incurred up to the date of disposition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for felony animal cruelty because the statute was not unconstitutionally vague where it gave fair notice of the prohibited conduct. Defendant was responsible for all costs of impoundment from the time of seizure to the time of proper disposition. The court reversed the misdemeanor animal neglect conviction because the trial court failed to give an instruction on criminal negligence.

 

599 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



S. Cal. Labor Mgmt. Operating Eng'Rs Contract Compliance Comm. v. Aubry


No. A074161.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


54 Cal. App. 4th 873; 63 Cal. Rptr. 2d 106; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 347; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3259; 97 Daily Journal DAR 5688



March 31, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing April 29, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 348. The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed from Unpublished to Published April 29, 1997. Review Denied July 9, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 4197.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court for the city and County of San Francisco, No. 974794, William J. Cahill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs are awarded to respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contract compliance committee sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied appellants' petition for a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent Director of the Department of Industrial Relations of the State of California to issue a directive that a construction project was subject to California's Prevailing Wage Law, Cal. Lab. Code §§ 1720-1781.


OVERVIEW: Appellant contract compliance committee contended that California's Prevailing Wage Law (PWL), Cal. Lab. Code §§ 1720-1781, applied to the construction of a dam project that was awarded by an agency of the federal government, and sought judicial intervention when respondent Director of the Department of Industrial Relations of the State of California refused to apply PWL to the project. The construction of the dam was governed by a local cooperation agreement between the Army and local flood control districts. Federal funds paid about 50 percent of the costs. Cal. Code Regs. tit. 8 § 16001, provided that PWL applied when projects were controlled by state-awarding bodies. The trial court denied appellants' request for a writ on mandate, and upon review, the court affirmed. The court held that even where congress has not completely displaced state regulation in a specific area, state law was nullified to the extent that it conflicted with federal law. The court found that it was impossible to apply both laws and that the Davis-Bacon Act, 40 U.S.C.S. § 276a, preempted the PWL.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying appellants', contract compliance committee, writ of mandate was affirmed because the dam construction project was a federally controlled project and therefore the Davis-Bacon Act preempted the California Prevailing Wage Law.

 

600 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Graham


F024209


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


53 Cal. App. 4th 1288; 62 Cal. Rptr. 2d 336; 1997 Cal. App. LEXIS 243; 97 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2420; 97 Daily Journal DAR 4343



March 28, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted June 24, 1997 (S061117), Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 3704.
Review Dismissed September 24, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Cal. LEXIS 5884.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County.. (Super. Ct. No. 533472-7). John E. Fitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The sentence imposed is vacated and the matter remanded to the trial court for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which denied his motion to strike a prior juvenile conviction for the purposes of its use as a "strike" under Cal. Penal Code § 667 et seq., and sentenced him to 25 years to life for his conviction of being an ex-felon in possession of a firearm, brandishing a firearm, and being under the influence of a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of being an ex-felon in possession of a firearm, brandishing a firearm, and being under the influence of a controlled substance. Prior to sentencing he filed a motion to strike the use of his juvenile conviction for purposes of its use as a "strike" or prior felony under the three strikes law, Cal. Penal Code § 667 et seq. The lower court denied the motion and imposed the maximum sentence. On review the court affirmed. The court ruled that the lower court did not err in using appellant's juvenile adjudication as a prior felony under the three strikes law. The court maintained that an express finding of appellant's fitness was made by operation of juvenile court law; thus, his juvenile adjudication could be used as a "strike," and there was no constitutional requirement that a jury trial be held in order for a juvenile conviction to be used for sentence enhancement. The court held that using a juvenile conviction to enhance appellant's sentence was a foreseeable judicial use of such and did not violate his due process rights. Appellant's juvenile conviction met the requirements of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(b), and was properly used as a "strike."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of being an ex-felon in possession of a firearm, brandishing a firearm, and being under the influence of a controlled substance, because the lower court properly denied appellant's motion to strike the use of his prior juvenile conviction for the purposes of its use as a "strike" under the three strikes law.
